Agent Selling Half of a Slave: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Analyzing the validity of an agent selling half of a slave when appointed to sell the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on an agent selling half of a slave, distinguish the ruling of Abu Hanifa from the Sahibayn, and identify the mufta bihi ruling and its condition.

Core Idea
Selling half of a slave by an agent is valid only if the other half is sold before dispute.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding agency in sale. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that an agent selling half of a slave is valid unconditionally, while the two pupils (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) restrict validity unless the agent also sells the other half before any dispute arises. Understanding this ikhtilaf and its resolution is key to grasping how the Hanafi school handles competing proofs based on textual absoluteness versus customary practice (urf) and harm prevention.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: the sale of half is valid unconditionally. His evidence: the wording is absolute (mutlaq) about selling the slave, not restricted to selling all together. Analogy: selling whole for half price is valid, so selling half is a fortiori valid.
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
State the Sahibayn's position: the sale is not valid because it is not customary (ghayr muta'arif) and causes harm of co-ownership (shirkah). However, they allow an exception: if the agent sells the other half before dispute, the first sale becomes valid as a means (wasila).
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof and istihsan
The Sahibayn base their ruling on custom (urf) and prevention of harm. Their istihsan (juristic preference) recognizes that selling half may be a means to comply with the order if the buyer cannot be found for the whole. The condition of selling the other half before dispute proves the means was genuine.
4
Determine the tarjih and mufta bihi
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah prefers the Sahibayn's view. The fatwa (operative ruling) is according to them: the sale is invalid unless the other half is sold before dispute. This is the relied-upon position in the madhhab.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In practice, an agent cannot simply sell half of a slave. If he does, the sale is void unless he immediately sells the remaining half, thereby showing it was a means to fulfill the principal's command. This protects the principal from unwanted co-ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء الناس فيه، ووجهه أن التصرف يكثر وجوده في العروض ويقل في العقار ويتوسط في الحيوان وكثرة الغبن لقلة التصرف ا هـ . والمراد بالتغابن الخداع فقولهم لا يتغابن الناس فيه معناه لا يخدع بعضهم بعضاً لفحشه وظهوره، وقولهم يتغابن الناس فيه أي يخدع بعضهم بعضاً لقلته. قال في القاموس : غبنه في البيع يغبنه غبناً ويحرك خدعه والتغابن أن يغبن بعضهم بعضاً ا هـ . وعلى هذا فقولهم غبن فاحش أي خداع قوله : (ولو وكل ببيع عبد فباع نصفه (صح) أي عند أبي حنيفة لأن اللفظ مطلق عن قيد الافتراق والاجتماع ألا ترى أنه لو باع الكل بثمن النصف يجوز عنده فإذا باع النصف أولى. وقالا : لا يجوز لأنه غير متعارف لما فيه من ضرر الشركة إلا أن يبيع النصف الآخر قبل أن يختصما لأن بيع النصف قد يقع وسيلة إلى الامتثال بأن لا يجد من يشتريه جملة فيحتاج إلى أن يفرق وإذا باع الباقي قبل نقض البيع الأول تبين أنه وقع وسيلة، وإذا لم يبع ظهر أنه لم يقع وسيلة فلا يجوز، وهذا استحسان عندهما؛ كذا في الهداية وهو يفيد ترجيح قولهما ولذا أخره مع دليله كما هو عادته، ولذا استشهد لقول الإمام بما لو باع الكل بثمن النصف فإنه يجوز ، وقد علمت أن المفتى به خلاف قوله. وفي الخزانة : أمر ببيع عبده بألف فباع نصفه بألف جاز بيعه بألف وقد أحسن وإن باع نصفه بألف إلا درهماً وكر حنطة بطل ا هـ . والمراد من العبد ما في تبعيضه ضرر احترازاً عما لا ضرر في تبعيضه كالحنطة والشعير فيجوز بيع البعض اتفاقاً؛ كذا في المعراج . وفي البزازية وكله ببيع عبدين فباع أحدهما جاز إن لم يكن فيه ضرر، وإن أحدهما أجود فعلى الخلاف. وكله ببيعهما بألف فباع أحدهما بأربعمائة إن كان ذلك حصة من الثمن أو أكثر جاز، وإن أقل فلا عند الأمام. وقالا : إن قدر ما يتغابن جاز ا هـ
Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. The people in it, and its explanation: that transactions occur frequently in movable goods, rarely in real estate, and moderately in animals; and excess of ghabn (deception) is due to lack of transaction. The meaning of taghabun is deception. So their statement 'people do not practice taghabun regarding it' means they do not deceive each other due to its flagrancy and obviousness; and their statement 'people practice taghabun regarding it' means they deceive each other due to its rarity. He said in al-Qamus: he deceived him in a sale, he deceived him, ghabnan (with a fatha), and with a sukun, meaning he cheated him; and taghabun is that some deceive others. Based on this, their statement 'ghabn fahish' means gross deception. (His statement: 'And if he appointed him to sell a slave and he sold half of it, it is valid') i.e., according to Abu Hanifa because the wording is absolute without restriction of separation or joining. Do you not see that if he sold the whole for the price of half, it is valid according to him, so selling half is more appropriate. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is not permissible because it is not customary, due to the harm of co-ownership, unless he sells the other half before they dispute, because selling half may occur as a means to compliance if he cannot find someone to buy it in bulk, so he needs to divide it. If he sells the remainder before the first sale is contested, it becomes clear that it was a means; if he does not sell, it becomes clear that it was not a means, so it is not permissible. This is istihsan according to them. Thus in al-Hidayah, and it indicates the preference of their view, and therefore he delayed it with its proof as is his habit, and therefore he used as evidence for the Imam's statement that if he sold the whole for half the price, it is valid, and you know that the fatwa is contrary to his statement. In al-Khazanah: He ordered the sale of his slave for one thousand, so he sold half of it for one thousand; his sale is valid for one thousand and he has done well. But if he sold half for one thousand minus one dirham and a kurr of wheat, it is invalid. The intended meaning of 'slave' is that dividing it causes harm, as opposed to what does not cause harm in its division, like wheat and barley, so selling part of it is valid by agreement. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: He appointed him to sell two slaves and he sold one of them; it is valid if there is no harm in it, but if one of them is of better quality, then there is a difference of opinion. And he appointed him to sell both for one thousand, so he sold one for four hundred; if that is the share of the price or more, it is valid; if less, then according to the Imam it is not valid. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: if it is within the amount that people overlook, it is valid. End.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding an agent appointed to sell a slave who sells only half of it. The text first defines 'ghabn fahish' (gross deception) as a type of cheating that is so obvious that people do not normally engage in it. Then it presents the ruling: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, selling half of the slave is valid because the command to sell is absolute, not restricted to selling the whole together. He argues by analogy: if selling the whole for half the price is valid, selling half is even more appropriate. The two imams, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that it is not valid because it is not customary (ghayr muta'arif) and it imposes the harm of co-ownership. However, they make an exception: if the agent sells the other half before the parties dispute, it becomes a means (wasilah) to fulfill the order, and the sale is valid from the beginning. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The author of al-Hidayah presents the Sahibayn's view as the mufta bihi (relied-upon position). The passage then provides additional examples from other works: if the agent sells half for exactly half the price, it is valid; if he sells it for less, it is invalid. Also, if the agent is appointed to sell two slaves and sells one, it is valid if there is no harm, but if one is of better quality, there is a dispute. The key point is that the fatwa is against Abu Hanifa, meaning the operative ruling follows the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghabn fāḥish): Gross deception in a transaction, so obvious that people normally do not engage in it.
متعارف (mutaʿārif): Customary practice recognized by people in transactions.
وسيلة (wasīla): A means to achieve a legal objective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the agent sells the other half before the parties dispute
B) If the agent sells the other half after the dispute
C) If the slave is worth less than the full price
D) If the agent obtains the principal's consent after the sale

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi ruling is that an agent cannot sell half of a slave unless he also disposes of the other half before any dispute arises, preventing unwanted co-ownership.

Agent's Purchase of Half a Slave: Suspended Until Completion
Explaining that an agent's purchase of half a slave is suspended until he buys the rest by agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that an agent's purchase of half a slave is suspended until the remainder is bought, and identify the logical basis (wasilah) for this consensus.

Core Idea
An agent's purchase of half a slave is suspended by consensus; it becomes binding upon completion.

In this passage, Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if an agent appointed to buy a slave purchases only half, the contract is not invalid but suspended (mawquf) by agreement of the Hanafi imams. The suspension is resolved if the agent buys the remaining half before the principal rejects the initial purchase. This ruling highlights the Hanafi concern for practicality (as a means to acquire inherited shares) and the distinction between buying and selling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The agent is appointed to buy a slave but buys only half. Determine what the ruling is.
2
State the ruling
The purchase of half is suspended (mawquf) by agreement of the Hanafi authorities.
It is not void; it is conditional.
3
Understand the condition for binding
The suspension is lifted if the agent buys the remaining half before the principal rejects the first purchase.
If the principal rejects first, the first purchase becomes void.
4
Analyze the rationale
Buying part may be a means (wasilah) to fulfill the command, e.g., when the slave is inherited by multiple people and the agent must buy shares one by one.
5
Compare with selling
Contrast with selling: an agent appointed to sell can sell half immediately because he acts on the principal's property; buying involves another's property, hence restriction.
The difference is due to ownership and potential tuhma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي الشراء يتوقف ما لم يشتر الباقي يعني لو وكله بشراء عبد فاشترى نصفه فالشراء موقوف اتفاقاً فإن اشترى باقيه لزم الموكل لأن شراء البعض قد يقع وسيلة إلى الامتثال بأن كان موروثاً بين جماعة فيحتاج إلى شرائه شقصاً شقصاً، فإذا اشترى الباقي قبل رد الآمر البيع تبين أنه وسيلة فينفذ على الأمر وهذا بالاتفاق، والفرق لأبي حنيفة أن في الشراء تتحقق التهمة على ما مر وآخر أن الأمر بالبيع يصادف ملكه فيصح فيعتبر فيه إطلاقه والآمر بالشراء صادف ملك الغير فلم فلا يعتبر فيه التقييد والإطلاق.
His statement: (And regarding purchase, it is suspended until he buys the remainder), meaning if he appointed him to buy a slave and he bought half of it, the purchase is suspended by agreement. If he buys the rest of it, it becomes binding on the principal, because buying part may occur as a means to compliance, as if it is inherited among a group and he needs to buy it share by share. So if he buys the remainder before the principal rejects the sale, it becomes clear that it is a means, and it is executed upon the principal by agreement. The distinction for Abu Hanifa is that in purchasing, the suspicion (tuhma) is realized as previously mentioned, and another point is that the command to sell encounters his ownership, so it is valid and its absoluteness is considered, while the command to purchase encounters the ownership of another, so it is not considered with respect to restriction and absoluteness.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for an agent appointed to buy a slave who buys only half. By consensus of the Hanafi school, the purchase is suspended (mawquf) until the agent buys the remaining half. If he does buy the rest before the principal rejects the first purchase, the entire purchase becomes binding on the principal. The reason is that buying a part may be a means (wasilah) to fulfill the order, for example when the slave is inherited by multiple people and the agent needs to acquire shares one by one. The text also gives the reasoning of Abu Hanifa for the difference between selling and buying: in selling, the agent acts on the principal's property, so the command is absolute; in buying, the agent deals with another's property, so the command is not absolute and restrictions apply. The key point is that the purchase is not immediately effective but becomes effective upon completion, and this is agreed upon among the Hanafi imams.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended: the contract is not immediately effective but becomes effective upon fulfillment of a condition
وسيلة (wasīla): A means to fulfill the command
تهمة (tuhma): Suspicion that the agent might act for his own benefit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon buying half
B) When the agent buys the remaining half before the principal rejects the first purchase
C) When the principal ratifies the purchase
D) One month after the first purchase

Key Takeaway
An agent's purchase of half a slave is suspended; it becomes binding only when the agent buys the remaining half before the principal can reject, reflecting the Hanafi principle that incomplete acts may be means (wasila) to the goal.

Agent's Compulsion to Accept Defective Item Return: Evidence, Nukool, and Defect Types
Figh ruling with proofs: agent's compulsion in nukool and defect return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an agent is compelled to accept a returned defective item and explain how the type of defect and evidence affect liability.

Core Idea
An agent must accept a defective item return when the buyer provides evidence or the agent refuses an oath, or for defects that could not have occurred after sale.

This lesson analyzes the agent's position when a buyer returns a defective item. The agent is not always liable; the ruling depends on the type of defect and the evidence provided. We will explore the Hanafi rules on evidence (bayyinah), refusal to swear (nukool), and confession (iqrar), and how they interact with different defect categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agent's core obligation
The agent is bound to accept the return if the buyer proves the defect existed at sale via evidence or nukool, or for defects that cannot occur later.
This is the baseline ruling, but exceptions exist based on defect type.
2
Classify the defect type
Defects are classified as non-incident (e.g., extra finger), incident but non-recurring in that period, or incident and recurring. Each has different evidentiary requirements.
Non-incident defects require no proof; incident recurring defects require evidence or nukool.
3
Apply the evidence hierarchy
Bayyinah is absolute proof; nukool compels the agent; iqrar is deficient and only binds for non-incident defects.
Iqrar does not bind for recurring defects because the judge cannot be certain the defect existed at sale.
4
Distinguish return by court order vs. without
Return by court order is annulment (faskh) and allows the agent to litigate against the principal; without court order it is a new sale, so the agent cannot litigate.
This affects the agent's recourse and the final liability.
5
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The agent swears on knowledge (not certainty) per the majority narrations; the fatwa follows this view.
The intra-Hanafi difference does not affect the practical outcome in most cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (And the agent is compelled in the nukool). Al-Ramli said: In it is evidence that if the claim occurred regarding the price of the sold item, in that the buyer claimed he paid the agent and the agent denied it, so the buyer requested his oath that he did not pay, and he (the agent) refused (nakala), then the judge ruled that he must guarantee the price to the principal due to the absence of the mentioned cause, and because he is either paying or admitting, and in both cases he guarantees. This is the fatwa case. I say: In al-Kifayah, his statement 'the agent is compelled' indicates that the agent takes an oath of certainty (qat'iyyah), for if it were on knowledge, he would not be compelled, due to the remoteness of the defect from his knowledge. But the majority of narrations are that the agent swears on knowledge, so if he knows of the defect, then he is compelled to nukool. His statement: (And if the buyer returned the sold item to the agent due to a defect with evidence (bayyinah) or nukool, he returns it to the principal; likewise with iqrar (confession) in what does not occur similarly) because evidence is an absolute proof, and the agent is compelled in nukool due to the remoteness of the defect from his knowledge, considering his lack of familiarity with the item, so it binds the principal. Likewise with iqrar in what does not occur similarly, because the judge is certain that the defect occurred in the seller's hands, so his judgment is not based on these proofs. The interpretation of their stipulation in the book is that the judge knows that it does not occur in, for example, a month, but he is uncertain about the date of sale, so he needs these proofs to reveal the date, or it was a defect known only by women and doctors, and their statements are proof for directing the litigation but not for the return, so he needs them for the return. Even if the judge witnessed the sale and the defect is apparent, he does not need any of them. He restricted it to what does not occur, because if he returned it by his iqrar regarding what does occur, then it binds the agent, because iqrar is a deficient proof and he is not compelled to it since he could remain silent or refuse. However, he may litigate against the principal and bind him with evidence or his nukool, unlike if the return is without a court order and the defect does occur similarly, where he cannot litigate against his seller because it is a new sale regarding a third party and the seller is the third of them. The return by court order is annulment (faskh) due to the generality of the judge's authority; but the deficient proof, i.e., iqrar, from the perspective of annulment, he may litigate, but from the perspective of deficiency, it does not bind the principal except with proof, even if the defect does not occur similarly. Return without a court order by his iqrar binds the principal without litigation in one narration because the return is certain; but in most narrations, he cannot litigate for the reason mentioned. He answered with 'the interpretation of their stipulation' etc. In Sharh al-Zayla'i: The gist is that the defect is either not incident (like extra tooth or finger), or it is incident but does not occur similarly in such a period, or it does occur similarly. In the first, the judge returns it without any proof (bayyinah, nukool, or iqrar). Similarly in the second due to his knowledge that it was with the seller, and the interpretation of stipulating proof as mentioned by the author here. Similarly the ruling in the third if it is with evidence or nukool, because evidence is an absolute proof and likewise nukool is proof against him, so he returns it. Then in all these cases, the judge's return to the agent is a return to the principal. Then he mentioned the ruling of return in this third case by iqrar with and without a court order, and the ruling of return in the first two by iqrar without a court order, and it will come in the author's speech. Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage discusses the agent's liability when a buyer returns a defective item and the agent fails to take an oath (nukool) or provides other evidence. It opens by citing al-Ramli: if the buyer claims he paid the agent and the agent denies it, then refuses to swear, the agent must compensate the principal because he is either admitting or failing to disprove. The main ruling: if the buyer returns the item to the agent due to a defect and provides evidence (bayyinah) or the agent refuses an oath (nukool), the agent must accept the return and pass it on to the principal. Similarly, if the agent confesses (iqrar) for a defect that could not have occurred after sale, the judge is certain it existed at the time of sale, so the return is binding. The commentary then distinguishes three types of defects: (1) non-incident defects (like extra finger) — the judge orders return without any proof; (2) incident defects that cannot recur in that period — same ruling, but proof may be needed if the sale date is uncertain; (3) incident defects that can recur — return is only with evidence or nukool, not with iqrar alone because iqrar is a deficient proof and the agent could remain silent. The passage also explains that if the return is by court order (qada), it is an annulment (faskh) and the agent can litigate against the principal; if without court order, the agent may not litigate because it is a new sale. Ultimately, the right is first to insist on soundness, then to return, then to seek compensation for reduction; so the return is not definite. The teacher (al-Zayla'i) clarifies that all these rulings apply only to the agent, and the return to the agent is effectively a return to the principal.

Key Terms
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to take an oath after being asked to swear
بينة (bayyina): Evidence, typically testimony of two witnesses
إقرار (iqrār): Confession or admission
عيب حادث (ʿayb ḥādith): A defect that occurs after the sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case does a judge order the return of a defective item without requiring evidence (bayyinah or nukool)?
A) When the defect could have occurred after sale
B) When the defect is not incident (e.g., extra finger)
C) When the agent confesses to the defect
D) When the buyer provides a medical report

Key Takeaway
The agent's liability to accept a defective item return depends on the defect type and the strength of the buyer's proof; for non-incident defects, no proof is needed.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Return of Defective Goods to the Agent: When Does It Bind the Principal?
Explains when a buyer's defect return to the agent binds the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a buyer's return of defective goods to the agent binds the principal, and distinguish between the effects of evidence (bayyinah), refusal to swear (nukul), and acknowledgment (iqrar), as well as identify the intra-Hanafi dispute for non-recurring defects.

Core Idea
An agent's acceptance of a defect return binds the principal only with absolute proof (bayyinah) or compelled refusal (nukul); acknowledgment (iqrar) binds only the agent unless the defect is non-recurring, and even then there is a dispute.

When goods are purchased through an agent and later found defective, the buyer may seek to return them. But does returning them to the agent automatically obligate the principal? The answer hinges on the type of proof and the nature of the defect. This lesson unpacks the detailed ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, exploring the Hanafi distinction between absolute and limited proofs, and the intra-madhhab difference on acknowledgment for non-recurring defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three defect types
Classify the defect as (1) non-recurring (e.g., extra finger), (2) recurring but atypical (not typical in that time period), or (3) recurring typically. This determines the need for proof.
Non-recurring defects are those that cannot arise after the sale, like an extra finger or tooth.
2
Determine the legal proof used
The buyer may present evidence (bayyinah), the agent may refuse to swear (nukul), or the agent may acknowledge the defect (iqrar). Each proof has a different evidentiary weight in Hanafi law.
Bayyinah is absolute proof (hujjah mutlaqah), iqrar is limited proof (hujjah qasirah).
3
Apply the Hanafi hierarchy of proofs
For bayyinah and nukul, the agent is considered compelled (because he lacks personal knowledge), so the return binds the principal. For iqrar, the agent acts voluntarily, so the return binds only himself unless the defect is non-recurring.
This is based on the principle that the agent's compulsion (idtirar) causes the effect to pass to the principal.
4
Assess the intra-Hanafi dispute for non-recurring defects
When the defect does not recur and the agent acknowledges without a court order, the majority (al-Mabsut) say the return binds only the agent; a minority narration (al-Buyu') says it binds the principal. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The dispute centers on whether the return is 'determinate' (mutayyan) when the defect is obviously pre-existing.
5
Distinguish the practical consequences
Determine who bears the loss: if the return binds the principal, the principal must accept the return and reimburse the buyer; if it binds only the agent, the agent is personally liable. Also consider whether the return was by court order or mutual consent.
A return by court order is rescission (faskh) and binds both; a return without court order may be treated as a new sale.

الموكل لكان أولى لأن الوكيل لا يحتاج إلى خصومة مع الموكل إلا إذا كان غيباً يحدث مثله، ورد عليه بإقرار سواء كان بقضاء أو لا، لكن إن كان بقضاء احتاج إلى خصومة مع الموكل وإلا لا تصح خصومته لكونه مشترياً. وجعل النكول هنا بمنزلة البينة لا الإقرار ولم يجعل في حق البائع كذلك حتى لو رد على البائع بنكوله لا يرده على بائعه لاضطرار الوكيل إلى النكول بخلاف البائع؛ كذا في النهاية. وفيها: وقضاء القاضي مع إقرار الوكيل متصور فيما إذا أقر بالعيب وامتنع عن القبول فيقضى عليه جبراً على القبول ا أطلق في جواز الرد على الوكيل فشمل ما إذا كان قبل قبض الثمن أو بعده كما في البزازية. وأشار إلى أن الخصومة إنما هي مع الوكيل فلا دعوى للمشتري على الموكل فلو أقر الموكل بعيب فيه وأنكره الوكيل لا يلزم الوكيل ولا الموكل شيء لأن الخصومة فيه من حقوق العقد والموكل أجنبي فيه، ولو أقر الوكيل وأنكر الموكل رده المشتري على الوكيل وإقراره صحيح في حق نفسه لا الموكل؛ كذا في البزازية. ولم يذكر المؤلف الرجوع بالثمن وحكمه أنه يرجع به على الوكيل إن كان نقده الثمن وعلى الموكل إن كان نقده كما في شرح الطحاوي ولم يذكر ما إذا نقد الثمن إلى الوكيل ثم أعطاه هو إلى الموكل ثم وجد المشتري عيباً يرده على الوكيل أم الموكل؟ أفتى القاضي أنه يرده على الوكيل ؛ كذا في البزازية . وقيد بالوكيل بالبيع لأن الوكيل بالإجارة إذا أجر وسلم ثم طعن المستأجر فيه بعيب فقبل الوكيل بغير قضاء فإنه يلزم الموكل، ولم يعتبر إجارة جديدة في حق الموكل لأن المعقود عليه إن كان المنافع فهي غير مقبوضة فكان نظير الرد على الوكيل بالبيع قبل القبض وإن كان المعقود عليه العين باعتبار إقامتها مقام المنافع

Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase

The remainder: If the buyer returns the sold item to the agent due to a defect with evidence (bayyinah) or refusal to swear (nukul), he returns it to the principal. Likewise, [the author's] saying: "And if the buyer returns the sold item to the agent due to a defect with evidence or refusal to swear, he returns it to the principal; likewise with an acknowledgment (iqrar) for a defect that does not recur like it" because evidence is an absolute proof, and the agent is compelled in refusal to swear due to the defect being beyond his knowledge given his lack of familiarity with the item, so it binds the principal. Likewise with acknowledgment for what does not recur, because the judge is certain that the defect occurred in the seller's hand, so his judgment is not based on these proofs. The interpretation of requiring them in the book [Kanz al-Daqa'iq] is that the judge knows that it does not occur within a month, for example, but the date of sale is ambiguous, so these proofs are needed to clarify that date; or the defect is such that only women and doctors know it, and their statements are proof for directing the lawsuit but not for the return, so they are needed for the return. Even if the judge witnessed the sale and the defect is apparent, he needs none of this. He qualified it with "what does not recur" because if he returns it based on his acknowledgment of a recurring defect, it binds the agent, since acknowledgment is a limited proof and he is not compelled to it, being able to remain silent or refuse to swear, except that he may litigate the principal and bind him with evidence or his refusal to swear, unlike if the return is without a court order and the defect recurs, where he cannot litigate his seller because it is a new sale regarding a third party, and the seller is their third. The return by court order is a rescission due to the general authority of the judge, but the limited proof (acknowledgment) — from the perspective of rescission he may litigate, and from the perspective of limitation, the principal is not bound except with proof. And if the defect does not recur, the return without a court order by his acknowledgment binds the principal without litigation in one narration because the return is determined; but in the majority of narrations he cannot litigate for the reason we mentioned. The right is first in the description of safety, then it moves to return, then to recourse for diminution, so the return is not determined. If the author said in the response, it is a return...

...according to his certain knowledge?

The narrations [indicate] that the agent swears on knowledge; when he knows of the defect, then he is compelled to refuse to swear.

His saying: "So his judgment was not based on these proofs" is a response to a question: when the defect is of a type that does not recur, like an extra finger, the judge's decision should not depend on the existence of these proofs of evidence, acknowledgment, or refusal to swear; rather, he should rule for the return upon the existence of the defect with the seller without proofs. So why is it dependent on them? He answered with "And the interpretation of requiring them" etc. (from al-Nihaya, summarized).

In Sharh al-Zayla'i: The conclusion is that the defect is either not newly occurring, like an extra tooth or extra finger; or newly occurring but does not recur in such a period; or recurs in such a period. In the first, the judge returns without proof of evidence, refusal, or acknowledgment. Likewise in the second, due to his knowledge that it was with the seller, and the interpretation of requiring proof as the author mentioned. Likewise in the third if it is with evidence or refusal to swear, because evidence is absolute proof, and likewise refusal is proof against him, so he returns it upon him. Then in all these cases, the judge's return on the agent is a return on the principal (summarized). Then he mentioned the ruling of return in this third case with acknowledgment with and without a court order, and the ruling of return in the first two with acknowledgment without a court order, and it will come in the author's words.

His saying: "he may litigate (his seller)" i.e., his principal.

His saying: "And if the defect does not recur" — the expression of al-Zayla'i here is clearer: it is "and if the defect is not newly occurring, like an extra tooth, or is newly occurring but does not recur in that period, and he returns it on the agent with his acknowledgment without a court order, it binds the agent, and he cannot litigate the principal in the majority of narrations of al-Mabsut. And it is mentioned in al-Buyu' that it is a return on the principal because they did exactly what the judge would do if brought to him, as the judge would not require him to produce evidence nor to swear in this case, but would return it without proof, so the right is determined in return. We say: The return by mutual consent is a new sale regarding a third party, and the principal is their third, and we do not concede that the right is determined in return; rather, his right is first established in the description of safety, then if unable, it moves to return, then if return is prevented by occurrence of a defect or an increase, it moves to recourse for diminution, so the return is not determined. Thus the two narrations are mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir and elsewhere, and there is great difference between them, because it descends from binding to not litigating at all. The closest is to say it does not bind him, but he may litigate. End. From this it is known that the text's saying "Likewise with acknowledgment for what does not recur" — i.e., it binds the principal — is based on the narration of al-Buyu' which contradicts the majority of narrations of al-Mabsut that it binds the agent. Therefore, al-Mawahib said: "If he returns it for what does not recur with acknowledgment, it binds the agent; and binding the principal is a narration."

His saying: "And return upon him with acknowledgment whether with or without a court order" — the more correct and concise is to say: if with a court order, then his litigation is valid; otherwise, his litigation is not valid because he is a buyer.

End of page.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the intricate rulings of agency (wakalah) when a buyer discovers a defect in goods purchased through an agent and seeks to return them. The central question is: when does the return to the agent legally bind the principal (the seller who appointed the agent)?

The passage is an expansion of the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, clarifying the conditions under which evidence (bayyinah), refusal to swear (nukul), or acknowledgment (iqrar) by the agent or buyer affect the principal. The author first states the general rule: if the buyer returns the defective item to the agent based on evidence or the agent's refusal to swear (meaning the agent refuses to take an oath denying the defect), the return is effective against the principal. This is because evidence is an absolute proof, and the agent is considered compelled in his refusal to swear because he lacks personal knowledge of the defect, having not handled the goods closely. Similarly, if the defect is of a type that does not typically recur (e.g., an extra finger or tooth), and the buyer acknowledges it, the return also binds the principal, because the judge is certain the defect existed in the seller's hand.

The text then delves into the nuance of recurring vs. non-recurring defects, the role of the judge, and the difference between returns with and without a court order. It cites multiple Hanafi sources (al-Zayla'i, al-Nihaya, al-Bazzaziyya) to present both the majority view (from al-Mabsut) and a dissenting view (from al-Buyu'). The key dispute is whether, when the defect does not recur and the agent accepts the return based solely on the buyer's acknowledgment without a court order, the return binds the principal or only the agent. The majority says it binds only the agent (since acknowledgment is a limited proof and the agent could have remained silent), while a minority narration says it binds the principal because the right to return is determined.

The commentary explains that the underlying legal reasoning involves the hierarchy of remedies: the buyer's primary right is to receive goods free of defect (sifah al-salamah); if that is breached, he may return; and if return is impossible due to subsequent changes, he may claim compensation for the diminution. Thus, the return is not always 'determinate,' which affects whether the principal is bound.

Finally, the passage touches on practical aspects: who bears the cost of return (the agent if he received the price, the principal if he did), and the distinction between agency for sale and agency for lease (ijarah). The author concludes with a summary of the two narrations and notes that the strongest view is that in cases of non-recurring defects and acknowledgment without court order, the agent is bound, but he may litigate the principal (i.e., seek reimbursement from him).

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): The agent appointed by the principal to sell or buy goods.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
عيب (‘ayb): A defect in the sold item that was present before sale or arose thereafter, entitling the buyer to return or claim compensation.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to take an oath when required, which is treated as an admission against the refuser.
إقرار (iqrār): A confession or acknowledgment of a right or fact, binding only on the person making it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if a buyer returns a defective item to the agent based solely on the buyer's acknowledgment (iqrar) and the defect is of a type that does not recur (e.g., an extra finger), what is the ruling according to the majority of narrations (Zahir al-Riwayah)?
A) The return binds the principal (muwakkil) and the agent is not liable.
B) The return binds only the agent (wakil); the principal is not bound.
C) The return is invalid and the buyer must go to court.
D) The agent must swear an oath, after which the return is binding on the principal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a buyer's return of defective goods to an agent binds the principal only when the return is supported by absolute proof (bayyinah or compelled nukul) or, for non-recurring defects, by a disputed minority view; otherwise, the agent bears the loss.

Dispute Between Principal and Agent on Sale Terms
Ruling with proof (talil) on principal-agent disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the principal's word is taken in disputes over terms of agency, and apply it to scenarios involving price, credit, and specification.

Core Idea
When principal and agent disagree on sale terms, the principal's word prevails—authority flows from him.

Agency (wakala) is built on the principal's command. When the agent acts contrary to instructions, or when there is a dispute about what was instructed, the court must decide whose word to accept. This lesson explains the default Hanafi rule: because the command originates from the principal, his claim about its content is credited unless proven otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the dispute is between the principal (al-muwakkil) and the agent (al-wakil) regarding the terms of the sale – e.g., cash vs. credit, specific price, buyer, or commodity.
The agent may claim absolute authority (itlaq); the principal claims restriction (taqyid).
2
State the ruling
The text rules: 'al-qawl li-l-amir' – the word is the principal's. This applies to all terms: price, payment method, buyer, etc.
The ruling is not limited to sale; it extends to marriage, hire, manumission, etc.
3
Understand the talil (legal reasoning)
The reason given: 'because the command is derived from him and there is no indication of absoluteness' – i.e., the principal is the source of authority, and without evidence of absolute delegation, the default is limited agency.
This is a principle of Hanafi usul: al-asl fi al-amr al-taqyid (the default in a command is restriction).
4
Apply to examples from the text
Work through the examples: agent sells for 500 but principal claims he ordered 1000; agent sells on credit but principal claims cash; agent sells without guarantor but principal required one. In all, the principal's word is taken.
The commentator notes that the matn's wording could have been broader to cover all types of specification.

Source Text

قوله : (إن باع نسيئة فقال أمرتك بنقد وقال المأمور أطلقت فالقول للآمر لأن الأمر يستفاد من جهته ولا دلالة على الإطلاق. وفي كافي الحاكم: وإذا باع الوكيل العبد بخمسمائة فقال الآمر أمرتك بألف وقال أمرتك بدينار أو بحنطة أو شعير أو باعه بنسيئة فقال أمرتك بالحال فالقول قول الآمر ، وكذلك هذا في النكاح والمكاتب والإجارة والعتق على مال ا هـ . ثم قال : ولو أمره أن يبيعه من فلان بكفيل فباعه بغير كفيل لم يجز، وإن قال الوكيل لم يأمرني بذلك فالقول للآمر ا هـ . فلو قال المؤلف لو اختلفا فيما عينه الموكل فالقول له لكان أولى ليشمل وكيل البيع والنكاح والإجارة والخلع والإعتاق والكتابة والمقدار والصفة من حلول وتأجيل والتقييد المقيد بمشتر ورهن وكفيل ووقت وقولي فيما عينه الموكل شامل لما إذا ادعى الموكل التقييد والوكيل الإطلاق، وما إذا ادعى الموكل تعيين شيء وادعى الوكيل تعيين آخر.
He said: (If he sold on credit, and he said I ordered you to sell for cash, and the agent said you gave me absolute authority, then the word is that of the commander because the command is derived from him and there is no indication of absoluteness. And in Kafi al-Hakim: If the agent sold the slave for five hundred, and the principal said I ordered you to sell for a thousand, and [the agent] said you ordered me to sell for a dinar or for wheat or barley, or he sold on credit and the principal said I ordered you to sell spot, then the word is that of the principal. And likewise in marriage, kitabah, hire, and manumission for payment. Then he said: If he ordered him to sell to so-and-so with a guarantor, and he sold without a guarantor, it is not permissible; and if the agent said he did not order me to do that, the word is that of the principal. So if the author said: 'If they differ regarding what the principal specified, the word is his, it would be better to include the agent for sale, marriage, hire, khul', manumission, kitabah, amount, and description such as deferred or immediate, and restrictions by buyer, pledge, guarantor, time, and my saying regarding what the principal specified includes the case where the principal claims restriction and the agent claims absoluteness, and the case where the principal claims specification of something and the agent claims specification of another.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (talil) from the commentary on the matn. It deals with disputes between the principal (al-muwakkil) and the agent (al-wakil) regarding the terms of sale. The rule is that the principal's word is accepted because the command originates from him; there is no presumption of absolute authority. The text cites examples from Kafi al-Hakim: if the agent sells for a different price, commodity, or on credit contrary to instructions, the principal's word is taken. This applies broadly to other contracts like marriage, kitabah, hire, and manumission for money. The commentator suggests that the matn's wording could be improved to cover all types of agents and specifications. The lesson emphasizes the legal principle that in disputes over the scope of authority, the principal's version prevails because he is the source of the command.

Key Terms
الإطلاق (al-itlaq): Absolute authority given to the agent without specifying terms
التقييد (al-taqyid): Restriction or specification by the principal on the agent's authority (e.g., cash only, specific buyer)

When Is a Sale Agent Believed?
Rulings on agent's claim of sale and payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a sale agent is believed regarding his claim of sale and receipt of payment, and apply these rules to given scenarios.

Core Idea
An agent's claim of sale and receipt is believed only if the sold item was in his possession; if consumed, istihsan applies.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the procedural rules for an agent who claims to have sold an item and received payment, in the face of the principal's denial. The text distinguishes between cases where the sold item is still extant or consumed, and whether the principal is alive or dead. It also addresses the effect of revocation of agency and extends the principles to other types of agents, such as those for manumission and kitabah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Variable
The primary factor determining whether the agent is believed is whether the sold item was in his possession (musallaman fi yadihi) at the time of the claim.
Possession here refers to physical custody, not mere legal ownership.
2
Distinguish Between Extant and Consumed Item
If the item still exists, the agent is not believed without evidence. If the item has been consumed (e.g., the slave died), the agent is believed after taking an oath, by istihsan.
The basis for istihsan is the agent's trusteeship (amanah) and the difficulty of proving destruction.
3
Consider the Principal's Status
If the principal is dead and his heirs deny the sale, the same rule applies: no belief without evidence if the item is extant; if consumed, the agent is believed with an oath.
The buyer's confirmation of the agent's claim does not change the rule against the principal.
4
Apply to Revocation Cases
If the principal claims he revoked the agency before the sale, the agent is not believed unless the buyer supports the agent's claim and the sale is then valid.
The agent's acknowledgment of a sale to a specific person, combined with the buyer's claim, overrides the principal's denial of agency.
5
Extend to Other Agents
The text notes analogous rules: an agent for manumission is not believed if the principal denies, but an agent for sale is believed. For an agent for kitabah, he is believed about the contract but not about receiving payment, unless he claims to have handed it over.
These distinctions rest on the nature of the agent's task and the ease of verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد الاختلاف في الإطلاق والتقييد لأن الوكيل بالبيع إذا ادعى البيع وقبض الثمن وهلاكه وادعاه المشتري وكذبهما الآمر فالوكيل يصدق مع يمينه فإن كان الأمر قد مات فقال ورثته لم يبعه وقال الوكيل قد بعته من فلان بألف وقبضت الثمن وهلك وصدقه المشتري، فإن كان العبد قائماً بعينه لم يصدق الوكيل على البيع إلا أن تقوم بينة أنه باعه في حياة الأمر، فإن لم تكن له بينة رد البيع وضمن الوكيل المال للمشتري، وإن كان العبد مستهلكاً فالوكيل مصدق بعد الحلف استحسن ذلك، وإن قال الأمر قد أخرجتك من الوكالة وقال الوكيل قد بعته أمس لم يصدق الوكيل ولو أقر الوكيل بالبيع لإنسان بعينه فقال الأمر قد أخرجتك من الوكالة جاز البيع إذا ادعى ذلك المشتري ؛ كذا في كافي الحاكم وإنما يصدق الوكيل في البيع وقبض الثمن وهلاكه عنده إذا كان المبيع مسلماً في يده، فإن كان في يد البائع فلا وتمامه في البزازية وفيها أيضاً وكيل العتق قال أعتقته أمس وكذبه موكله لا يعتق. وكيل البيع قال بعته أمس وكذبه موكله فالقول للوكيل الوكيل بالكتابة وقبض بدلها إذا قال كاتبت وقبضت بدلها فالقول له في الكتابة لا في قبض بدلها أما لو قال كاتبته ثم قال قبضت بدلها ودفعت إلى الموكل فهو صحيح مصدق لأنه أمين ا هـ . وتقدم الاختلاف بين وكيل الشراء وم وكله .
The difference is restricted to [the issue of] absoluteness and restriction, because the agent for sale, if he claims the sale and receipt of the price and its destruction, and the buyer claims it, and the principal denies both, then the agent is believed with his oath. If the principal has died and his heirs say he did not sell, and the agent says I sold it to so-and-so for a thousand and received the price and it perished, and the buyer confirms him, then if the slave is still in existence, the agent is not believed regarding the sale unless evidence is brought that he sold it during the principal's life; if he has no evidence, the sale is annulled and the agent is liable for the money to the buyer. If the slave has been consumed, the agent is believed after oath by istihsan. If the principal says I removed you from agency, and the agent says I sold it yesterday, the agent is not believed. If the agent acknowledges a sale to a specific person, and the principal says I removed you from agency, the sale is valid if the buyer claims that. As in Kafi al-Hakim. And the agent is only believed regarding the sale, receipt of price and its destruction when the sold item was in his possession; if it was in the seller's possession, then not. Its completion is in al-Bazzaziyya. In it also: An agent for manumission says I manumitted yesterday and his principal denies him, he is not manumitted. An agent for sale says I sold yesterday and his principal denies him, the word is the agent's. The agent for kitabah and receipt of its payment, if he says I made a kitabah contract and received its payment, he is believed regarding the kitabah but not regarding the receipt of payment. But if he says I made the kitabah contract then I received the payment and handed it to the principal, it is valid and he is believed because he is a trustee. And the difference between the agent for purchase and his principal has preceded.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (rational reasoning) covering a complex set of scenarios where the agent claims to have sold and received payment but the principal denies. The passage type is a ruling with detailed conditions. The key principle is that the agent's claim is accepted with his oath if the sold item was in his possession; if the item is still in the principal's possession, the agent is not believed. The text distinguishes between cases where the principal is alive or dead, and where the item is still extant or consumed. It also covers the effect of the principal's revocation of agency. Examples are given from Kafi al-Hakim and al-Bazzaziyya, including agents for manumission, sale, and kitabah. The lesson underscores that the agent's trustworthiness (amanah) is a factor, but not absolute.

Key Terms
صدق (saddaqa): To be believed or given credence in a legal dispute, often with an oath
أمين (amin): Trustee; an agent is considered an amin over the property entrusted to him

D) Never

Key Takeaway
An agent for sale is believed about his actions only when the sold item was in his possession; if consumed, istihsan permits belief with oath.

Agent for Sale Taking Pledge or Surety: No Liability for Loss
Expansion on the matn ruling for agent taking security

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the agent for sale taking a pledge or surety, explain why he is not liable for loss, and distinguish this case from the agent for debt collection.

Core Idea
The agent for sale acts as a principal; therefore, he may take security and bears no liability if it is lost.

An agent appointed to sell an item often needs to secure the price. He might take a pledge (rahn) or a surety (kafil) from the buyer. If the pledge is destroyed or the surety becomes uncollectible (tawa), is the agent liable? The Hanafi school provides a clear answer grounded in the agent’s capacity: the agent for sale acts as a principal (aseel) in receiving payment, so he may take security and is not liable for its loss. This stands in contrast to an agent for debt collection, who acts as a deputy and lacks such authority. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and related intra-Hanafi differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agents
Distinguish the agent for sale (وكيل بالبيع) from the agent for debt collection (وكيل بقبض الدين). The passage treats them differently regarding liability for lost security.
2
State the ruling for each type
The agent for sale is not liable if the pledge or surety is lost. The agent for debt collection, however, cannot validly take such security, and if he does, it does not affect the debt.
The passage reconciles conflicting texts by interpreting the surety taken with a condition freeing the debtor as hawalah.
3
Understand the ratio (talil)
The agent for sale is a principal (aseel) in receiving the price; he owns the act of receipt. The agent for debt collection is a deputy (na'ib) with limited authority from the principal.
This distinction is the key proof: the sale agent's action is his own, so he may take security; the debt collector's action is delegated, so he cannot exceed his mandate.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on returning the pledge
Most Hanafi authorities hold that the agent for sale may return the pledge, but he must guarantee the lesser of its value and the price. Abu Yusuf holds that returning is invalid.
This difference affects the agent's liability upon return.
5
Consider the conflict between al-Hidayah and other texts
Al-Hidayah says the agent for sale is not liable. Other works (al-Khulasah, al-Bazzaziyyah) say the agent for debt collection may take a surety, but this is resolved by interpreting that surety as hawalah.
The commentator explains that tawa (loss) attributable to taking the surety occurs only in specific contexts, e.g., under a Maliki judge who frees the original debtor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو أخذ الوكيل بالثمن رهناً فضاع أو كفيلاً فتوى عليه لا يضمن لأن الوكيل أصيل في الحقوق وقبض الثمن منها والكفالة توثق به والارتهان وثيقة لجانب الاستيفاء فيملكها بخلاف الوكيل بقبض الدين لأنه يفعل نيابة وقد أنابه في قبض الدين دون الكفالة وأخذ الرهن والوكيل بالبيع يقبض إصالة ولهذا لا يملك الموكل حجره عنه ؛ كذا في الهداية. وهذا مخالف لما في الخلاصة والبزازية من أن الوكيل بقبض الدين له أخذ الكفيل فيحمل كلام الهداية على أخذ الكفيل بشرط براءة الأصيل فإنها حينئذ حوالة وهو لا يملكها لما في البزازية. ولو أخذ كفيلاً بشرط البراءة فهو حوالة لا يجوز للوكيل بقبض الدين قبولها اهـ. ومن هنا قال صاحب النهاية : المراد بالكفالة هنا الحوالة لأن التوي لا يتحقق في الكفالة، وقيل الكفالة على حقيقتها لأن التوي يتحقق فيها بأن مات الكفيل والمكفول عنه مفلسين. قال الشارح أخذاً من الكافي : وهذا كله ليس بشيء لأن المراد هنا توى مضاف إلى أخذه الكفيل بحيث إنه لو لم يأخذ كفيلاً لم يتو دينه كما في الرهن والتوي الذي ذكره هنا غير مضاف إلى أخذه الكفيل بدليل أنه لو لم يأخذ كفيلاً أيضاً لتوى بموت من عليه الدين وحمله على الحوالة فاسد لأن الدين لا يتوى فيه بموت المحال عليه مفلساً بل يرجع به على المحيل وإنما يتوى بموتهما مفلسين فصار كالكفالة، والأوجه أن يقال المراد بالتوي توى مضاف إلى أخذ الكفيل وذلك يحصل بالمرافعة إلى حاكم يرى براءة الأصيل عن الدين بالكفالة ولا يرى الرجوع على الأصيل بموته مفلساً مثل أن يكون القاضي مالكياً ويحكم به ثم يموت الكفيل مفلساً اهـ. مسألة الكتاب أن أخذه الرهن يقع للموكل لكن لو رده الوكيل جاز ويضمن للموكل الأقل من قيمته ومن الثمن، وعند أبي يوسف لا يصح رده ؛ كذا ذكره التمرتاشي والمحبوبي؛ في المعراج والمراد بقوله لايضمن عدمه للموكل وإلا فالدين قد سقط بهلاك الرهن إذا كان مثل الثمن بخلاف الوكيل بقبض الدين إذا أخذ رهناً فضاع فإنه لا يسقط من دين الموكل شيء ولا ضمان على الوكيل كما في البزازية.
His saying: (And if the agent took a pledge for the price and it was lost, or a surety, then the fatwa is that he is not liable) because the agent is a principal (aseel) in rights and the receipt of the price from them, and the surety is a means of strengthening, and the pledge is a document for the side of recovery, so he possesses them, unlike the agent for debt collection because he acts as a deputy, and the principal appointed him for debt collection only, not for surety or taking pledge. And the agent for sale receives as a principal, and for this reason the principal cannot prevent him from that; as in al-Hidayah. And this contradicts what is in al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah that the agent for debt collection may take a surety. So the statement of al-Hidayah is interpreted as taking a surety with the condition of freeing the original debtor, because then it is a hawalah, and he does not own that as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And if he took a surety with the condition of freeing, it is hawalah, not permissible for the agent for debt collection to accept it. End. Hence, the author of al-Nihayah said: what is meant by surety here is hawalah because loss (tawa) does not occur in surety. And it was said that surety remains in its reality because loss occurs when the surety and the principal debtor both die insolvent. The commentator, taking from al-Kafi, said: all this is nothing because what is meant here is loss attributable to his taking the surety, such that if he had not taken a surety, his debt would not have been lost, like the pledge. And the loss mentioned here is not attributable to his taking the surety, because if he had not taken a surety, it would also have been lost by the death of the debtor. And interpreting it as hawalah is invalid because the debt is not lost in hawalah by the death of the assignee insolvent; rather, recourse is to the assignor. It is only lost by the death of both insolvent, so it becomes like surety. The most appropriate is to say that what is meant by loss is loss attributable to taking the surety, and that occurs by raising the matter to a judge who holds that the original debtor is freed from the debt by the surety and does not allow recourse to the original debtor if he dies insolvent, such as if the judge is a Malikite and so rules, then the surety dies insolvent. End. The mas'alah of the book is that his taking the pledge is for the principal, but if the agent returns it, he may do so, and he guarantees the lesser of its value and the price. According to Abu Yusuf, his return is not valid. As mentioned by al-Tamartashi and al-Mahbubi. In al-Mi'raj, and what is meant by his saying 'he does not guarantee' is its non-existence for the principal; otherwise, the debt has been discharged by the destruction of the pledge if it was equivalent to the price, unlike the agent for debt collection if he takes a pledge and it is lost, then nothing is discharged from the principal's debt and there is no guarantee on the agent, as in al-Bazzaziyyah.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn ruling about the agent who takes a pledge or surety for the sale price. The commentator, al-Zayla'i, explains that the agent for sale is not liable for loss because he acts as a principal (aseel) in receiving the price, thus he may take security. In contrast, the agent for debt collection acts as a deputy and cannot take security without explicit authorization. The passage then addresses a conflict between al-Hidayah and other works (al-Khulasah, al-Bazzaziyyah) and resolves it by stating that if the agent takes a surety with a condition freeing the original debtor, it becomes a hawalah (transfer of debt), which the agent cannot do. The discussion on the meaning of 'tawa' (loss) is examined: some say it is only possible in hawalah, but the preferred view is that loss occurs when the surety dies insolvent under a Malikite judge who frees the original debtor. The mas'alah of the book then states that the pledge taken by the agent belongs to the principal, but the agent may return it and will guarantee the lesser of its value and the price. Abu Yusuf holds that returning is invalid. The passage concludes with a comparison to the agent for debt collection, who if he takes a pledge and it is lost, nothing is discharged and he is not liable.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge taken by the agent as security for the sale price.
كفيل (kafil): A surety who guarantees payment of the debt.
حوالة (hawalah): Transfer of debt from one debtor to another, freeing the original debtor.
توى (tawa): Loss of the debt, i.e., inability to recover it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in al-Hidayah, is the agent for sale liable if the pledge he takes for the price is lost?
A) Yes, he is liable because he took the pledge.
B) No, he is not liable because he acts as a principal in receiving payment.
C) He is liable only if the pledge is worth more than the price.
D) He is liable only if the loss was due to his negligence.

Key Takeaway
The agent for sale who takes a pledge or surety for the price is not liable if it is lost, because he acts as a principal; this distinguishes him from the agent for debt collection.

Sale by Sub-Agent with Fixed Price: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegation of sale with fixed price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a sale by a delegated agent when the price is fixed, and identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
A sale by an unauthorized deputy is suspended until ratification — principal's consent to a fixed price does not itself authorize a sub-agent.

What happens when a principal appoints an agent to sell at a fixed price, and that agent delegates the sale to another? Does the sale become effective immediately, or does it depend on the principal's later approval? This passage from al-Khaniyah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on this very question, with Abu Yusuf holding the sale valid without ratification, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require ratification. The author, Ibn Nujaym, affirms the latter as the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case
Read the scenario: a principal appoints an agent to sell a specific garment for ten dirhams. The agent then delegates the task to another person, who sells the garment in the presence of the first agent.
The key elements: fixed price, delegation, presence of the first agent.
2
State the Positions
Note the three positions: Abu Yusuf holds the sale valid (yajuz) without ratification; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it not valid (la yajuz) without ratification; Ibn Abi Layla agrees with Abu Yusuf.
The presence or absence of the first agent does not change any position.
3
Analyze the Reasoning
Understand the logic: Ibn Abi Layla argues that the principal consented to the ownership transfer for that price. The Hanafi response (implied) is that consent to the price is not consent to any agent; the principal appointed only the first agent.
Abu Yusuf likely agrees with Ibn Abi Layla, but the text does not detail his reasoning.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Identify which view is relied upon in the school. Ibn Nujaym indicates that the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the correct one: the sale is suspended (mawquf) on ratification.
This means the principal can either ratify (making the sale valid) or reject (making it void).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية أيضاً: رجل وكل رجلاً أن له هذا الثوب بعشرة دراهم فوكل الوكيل بذلك غيره فباعه الثاني بحضرة الأول. روي عن أبي يوسف أنه يجوز هذا البيع كان الوكيل الأول حاضراً أو غائباً ولا يتوقف على الإجازة. وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد : لا يجوز كان الوكيل الأول حاضراً أو غائباً. وقال ابن أبي ليلى : يجوز كان الوكيل الأول حاضراً أو غائباً لأن الموكل رضي بزوال ملكه بالثمن المقرر .اهـ. فهو مؤيد لما قلناه فتدبر .
And in al-Khaniyah: A man appointed a man to [sell] him this garment for ten dirhams, then the agent delegated another for that, and the second sold it in the presence of the first. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that this sale is valid whether the first agent is present or absent, and it does not depend on ratification. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: It is not valid whether the first agent is present or absent. Ibn Abi Layla said: It is valid whether the first agent is present or absent, because the principal consented to the transfer of his ownership for the fixed price. End. So it supports what we said; reflect.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute and also the view of the early jurist Ibn Abi Layla. The case: a principal appoints an agent to sell a specific garment for ten dirhams. That agent appoints another agent, who then sells the garment in the presence of the first agent. Abu Yusuf holds the sale valid and effective immediately, regardless of whether the first agent is present or absent, and it does not require the principal's ratification. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagree, stating the sale is not valid, meaning it is suspended (mawquf) on ratification, whether the first agent is present or not. Ibn Abi Layla agrees with Abu Yusuf, arguing that the principal has already consented to the transfer of ownership at that exact price. The author (Ibn Nujaym) notes that this supports his earlier position that if the principal sets the price, the second agent may not sell without the first agent's presence or ratification. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, making the sale suspended on ratification.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkeel): Delegation of authority from one agent to another.
وكيل (wakeel): An agent appointed by the principal to act on his behalf.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or post-hoc approval of a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a principal says to an agent 'sell this garment for 10 dirhams', and the agent delegates to another who sells it in the presence of the first agent, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) The sale is valid and does not require ratification.
B) The sale is not valid unless ratified.
C) The sale is valid only if the first agent is present.
D) The sale is void automatically.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a sub-agent's sale with a fixed price is not effective without the principal's explicit permission or subsequent ratification, even if the first agent is present.

The Agent's Authority to Appoint a Sub-Agent (Tawkeel)
Explains validity and conditions of sub-agency in Hanafi law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where a sub-agent's action is immediately effective and where it requires ratification, and apply the Hanafi position on tawkeel.

Core Idea
An agent cannot delegate without permission; but purchase is an exception—sub-agent's action binds the first agent.

Agency (wakalah) is a contract built on trust. Can an agent hire another agent to do the job? The Hanafi school has a detailed answer that distinguishes between commercial transactions (like sale) and personal acts (like divorce). This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the sub-agent's action is immediately effective or requires the principal's ratification, and when the presence of the first agent substitutes for ratification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that an agent may not appoint a sub-agent without the principal's permission. This is the default rule.
Permission can be explicit or implicit through broad discretion.
2
Recognize the exceptions
List the exceptions: paying zakat, receiving debts for dependents, and purchase/sale. In these cases, the sub-agent's action is effective without prior permission.
The exception for purchase is that the sub-agent's act is immediately effective on the first agent.
3
Distinguish commercial vs personal acts
Understand that commercial contracts (sale) accept linkage to a condition and can be retroactively ratified, while personal acts (divorce, manumission) are tied to the person of the first agent and cannot be delegated.
Presence of the first agent may substitute for ratification in litigation but not in divorce.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that some Hanafi scholars (like those in al-Khaniyyah) hold that even sale is suspended on ratification, but the majority (jamahir) say sale is immediately effective on the first agent.
Al-Ramli provides a detail: if the sub-agent attributes the purchase to the principal, it is suspended; otherwise it is effective.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that ratification is required in all cases except purchase, where the sub-agent's action binds the first agent. The first agent then must ratify for the principal to be bound.
This is the view in al-Nihayah, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, and al-Khaniyyah.

Source Text

والخلع مع أنهما كالبيع كما في الخانية، فالعبارة الصحيحة ولا يوكل إلا بإذن إلا في دفع زكاة وقبض دين لمن في عياله وعند تقدير الثمن له والتفويض إلى رأيه كالإذن إلا في طلاق وعتاق فإن وكل بدونهما ففعل الثاني فأجازه الأول صح إلا في طلاق وعتاق وإبراء وخصومة وقضاء دين، وإن فعل أجنبي فأجازه الوكيل جاز إلا في شراء. وفي البزازية : قيل للوكيل اصنع ما شئت له التوكيل ولو قال الوكيل الأول ذلك لوكيله لا يملك Book of Agency, Chapter on Agency for Sale and Purchase: It is valid upon the principal. In al-Qunyah: If he appointed him to buy this slave for him, and the agent appointed a sub-agent, and he bought it, it falls for the first agent. If he said to him: 'Buy it for my principal', it falls for the second, and his appointment of a sub-agent is not valid with respect to himself nor his principal, end. This is interpreted as when the agent is absent, and its apparent meaning is that it does not depend on the ratification of the principal because it is a purchase by a party not authorized (fuduli), and that is not suspended. We previously mentioned from Adhiyat al-Khaniyyah that it is suspended, and in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that in purchase it is effective upon the first agent. He restricted it to contract to exclude the agent in divorce and manumission: if he appointed another, and the second divorced in the presence of a third-party agent, or a third-party divorced and the agent ratified, then it does not occur because the principal linked it to the word of the first, not the second, and it is dependent on a condition, unlike sale and similar. The commentators and Qadikhan restricted it to divorce and manumission, and release from debt is added, as in al-Qunyah: if he appointed him to release his debtor from debt, and the agent appointed a sub-agent, and he released him in the presence of the first, it is not valid, end. It should have been because it does not accept linkage to a condition, like sale, and litigation and payment of debt are added, so presence is not sufficient as in Sharh al-Majma', but it is contradicted in litigation by what is in al-Khaniyyah: if the second agent litigated and the principal was present, it is permissible because if the first is present, he litigated himself like an agent for sale, end. And the apparent meaning of what is in the book is that presence suffices without depending on ratification, and this is the opinion of some, and the majority is that ratification of the agent or principal is necessary, and the presence of the first agent is not sufficient, and absolute expressions are interpreted as requiring ratification; thus in al-Nihayah, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, and al-Khaniyyah. He said 'sold' and not 'contracted' to exclude purchase, for it is not suspended but is effective upon the third party as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, but it does not include marriage, kitabah, and khul', though they are like sale as in al-Khaniyyah. So the correct expression is: he may not appoint a sub-agent except with permission, except in paying zakat and receiving debt for those in his household, and when the price is estimated for him and discretion is delegated to him, it is like permission, except in divorce and manumission. If he appoints without these, and the second acts, and the first ratifies, it is valid, except in divorce, manumission, release, litigation, and payment of debt. If a third party acts and the agent ratifies, it is permissible, except in purchase. In al-Bazzaziyyah: It was said to the agent 'Do what you wish', he has the right to appoint a sub-agent. If the first agent said that to his agent, the second does not have the right to appoint a third. In al-Aqdiyyah: If the sultan said 'Appoint whoever you wish' and he appointed another, he said... (then the commentary with quotes from Ramli, etc.)
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi ruling on the validity of an agent appointing a sub-agent (tawkeel). The author begins with the general rule: if the principal authorizes a specific purchase (e.g., 'buy this slave'), and the agent appoints a sub-agent who buys it, the purchase falls for the first agent, not the principal. However, if the agent instructs the sub-agent to buy for the principal directly, it falls for the second (sub-agent) – but this is not valid for the sub-agent's own right or the principal's without proper authorization. The text then limits this to when the original agent is absent; otherwise, the presence of the first agent may change the ruling. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some say the sub-agent's purchase is suspended (mauquf) on the principal's ratification, while others say it is immediately effective (nafidh) on the first agent, especially in purchases (because a fuduli contract is not suspended in sale according to some). The discussion then distinguishes between commercial contracts (like sale) and personal acts (like divorce and manumission). For personal acts, if the agent appoints a sub-agent who divorces or manumits, it is invalid because the principal's right is tied to the first agent's action, and such acts are conditional (cannot be delegated retroactively). The text adds that release from debt, litigation, and payment of debt are similar to divorce and manumission in that presence alone is insufficient; ratification is needed. However, in litigation, if the principal is present, the second agent's action is valid as if the first litigated himself. The majority opinion (jumuhr) is that ratification is always required except in purchase where the sub-agent's action is effective on the first agent without ratification. The passage also covers cases where the principal gives broad discretion (e.g., 'do what you wish') – in that case, the agent may appoint a sub-agent, but that sub-agent cannot appoint a third unless explicitly authorized. Finally, the sultan's appointment of a deputy is considered like an agency, and if the agent says 'act according to your opinion', he may not appoint a sub-agent unless the principal allowed it.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent appointed by the principal (al-muwakkil) to perform a specific act.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): A person who acts without authorization on behalf of another; his contract is either suspended (mauquf) or immediately effective depending on the subject matter.
الحضرة (al-hadrah): Presence of the first agent (or principal) at the time of the sub-agent's action; sufficient without ratification in some cases (like litigation) but not in personal acts.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Ratification by the principal or first agent after the act; required for validity unless the act is immediately effective (like purchase).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, which of the following acts by a sub-agent is immediately effective on the first agent without ratification?
A) Divorce
B) Manumission
C) Purchase (sale)
D) Release from debt

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between commercial contracts (sale/purchase) and personal acts (divorce, manumission) regarding sub-agency: sale is immediately effective on the first agent; personal acts require ratification.
