Valid Boundaries for Real Estate in Hanafi Law
Ruling on valid property boundaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which natural and man-made features are valid as property boundaries according to the Hanafi madhhab, and apply the ruling when a boundary is defined by adjacency.

Core Idea
A road is always valid as a boundary without specifying dimensions; a wall is valid in the apparent madhhab, but rivers and ditches are disputed.

In property sales and legal claims, describing boundaries correctly is essential. The Hanafi school provides clear rulings on which natural or man-made features suffice as boundaries and whether any additional details are required. This lesson unpacks those rulings and addresses an important nuance when using adjacency to define a property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed features
Read the passage and list the four features mentioned: road, river, wall, and ditch. Note that the passage also discusses defining a property by adjacency to another person's land.
2
Determine the ruling for each feature
Classify each feature as valid, invalid, or disputed. The road is always valid. The river is not valid according to some. The wall is valid in the apparent madhhab (though there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that it is not). The ditch follows the same ruling as the river.
The phrase 'ظاهر المذهب' indicates the relied-upon position for the wall.
3
Understand the exception for road dimensions
Note that for a road, it is not necessary to specify its length and width, except for one opinion that requires it in cubits. The majority Hanafi view is that no such specification is needed.
This is the default rule; the exception is a minority view.
4
Analyze the adjacency rule
The passage states that if a property is defined as 'adjacent to the land of so-and-so' and that person owns multiple scattered lands in the same village, the description is still sufficient for a valid claim and testimony. This means the boundary is considered valid despite the neighbor's multiple parcels.
The key phrase is 'في هذه القرية التي فيها المدعاة أراض كثيرة متفرقة مختلفة' – it shows the diversity does not invalidate the description.
5
Summarize the practical outcome
Combine all the rulings: use a road or wall (according to the apparent madhhab) as a valid boundary without extra details; avoid rivers and ditches unless following the minority view; and when using adjacency, ensure the neighbor's identity is clear even if they have multiple lands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : الطريق يصلح حداً ولا حاجة فيه إلى بيان طوله وعرضه إلا على قول فإنه شرط أن يبينها بالذرع، والنهر لا يصلح حداً عند البعض وكذا السور وهو رواية عن أي حنيفة، وظاهر المذهب أنه يصلح حداً. والخندق كنهر ولو حد بأنه لزيق أرض فلان ولفلان في هذه القرية التي فيها المدعاة أراض كثيرة متفرقة مختلفة تصح الدعوى والشهادة.
Then he said: The road is valid as a boundary and there is no need to mention its length and width, except according to one opinion that requires specifying them in cubits. The river is not valid as a boundary according to some, and similarly the wall, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa. The apparent madhhab is that it is valid as a boundary. The ditch is like a river. And if he defines it as adjacent to the land of so-and-so, and so-and-so has in this village many scattered different lands, the claim and testimony are valid.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage lists the types of natural or man-made features that can serve as valid boundaries for defining a property in a legal claim or sale. Roads are always valid; one does not need to specify their length or width, though some scholars require that. Rivers are not valid according to some, and similarly walls; but the dominant view in the Hanafi madhhab is that they are valid. Ditches follow the same ruling as rivers. If a property is defined as adjacent to someone's land, even if that owner has many parcels, the description is sufficient for a valid claim and testimony.

Key Terms
يصلح حداً (yasluhu haddan): Valid as a boundary for defining property.
الخندق (al-khandaq): A ditch, treated like a river in boundary rulings.
ظاهر المذهب (zahir al-madhab): The apparent or predominant view of the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is stated to be valid as a boundary according to the apparent madhhab?
A) Road
B) River
C) Wall
D) Ditch

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, roads are always valid boundaries without dimension details; walls are valid in the apparent madhhab; rivers and ditches are disputed; and adjacency to a neighbor with multiple lands is sufficient if clearly stated.

The Necessity of Specifying Exceptions in a Sale Contract
Ruling on the need to clearly define exceptions in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on specifying exceptions in a sale and apply it to determine the validity of a contract.

Core Idea
Exceptions in a sale must be explicitly specified; mutual knowledge alone does not suffice.

In the sale of property, it is common for certain areas like mosques, graveyards, or public cisterns to be excluded from the transaction. But is it enough for both parties to know this informally, or must the contract spell it out? The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi position: explicit specification is necessary for legal clarity, especially for the judge who may later need to verify the sale. We explore the preferred view (mukhtar) and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is specifying exceptions (e.g., mosques, graveyards) in a sale contract. The ruling is wajib (necessary) for the sale to be valid.
This is not just recommended; it is a condition for validity.
2
Understand the reason for specification
Specification removes ambiguity and makes the sold item known to the judge at the time of testimony, which is required for legal enforcement.
The key phrase: 'فلا بد من التعيين' (so specification is necessary).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some earlier Hanafi masters considered specification unnecessary if both parties have full knowledge. However, the chosen view (mukhtar) is that it is required.
The author says 'وهو المختار' (and it is the chosen view).
4
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
When drafting a sale contract for a property that includes a mosque or public well, always explicitly except those areas in the contract. Failure to do so may render the sale invalid (batil).
This applies even if both parties are aware of the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : لا بد من تحديد المستثنيات من المساجد والمقابر والحياض العامة لتتميز وما يكتبون في زماننا وقد عرف المتعاقدان جميع ذلك وأحاطا به علماً فقد استرذله بعض مشايخنا وهو المختار إذ المبيع يصير به معلوماً للقاضي عند الشهادة فلا بد من التعيين ا هـ .
Then he said: It is necessary to specify the exceptions from mosques, graveyards, and public cisterns so that they are distinguished. And what they write in our time, when both contracting parties know all that and have full knowledge of it, some of our masters considered it base of him, and it is the chosen view because the sold item becomes known to the judge at the time of testimony, so specification is necessary. Ends.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of properties that include areas like mosques, graveyards, or public cisterns that are not actually part of the sale. The seller must explicitly exclude these areas in the contract. Even if both parties know these excluded portions, the contract must specify them because the judge needs clear identification when testimony is given later. Some later scholars considered it unnecessary if both parties are aware, but the preferred view (mukhtar) is that specification is mandatory.

Key Terms
المستثنيات (al-mustathnayat): Things excepted from the sale, like mosques or graveyards within the property bounds.
الحياض العامة (al-hiyad al-'ammah): Public cisterns or water reservoirs.
التحديد (al-tahdid): Specification or delimitation of the excluded items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it necessary to specify exceptions like mosques in a sale contract according to the chosen view?
A) Because the buyer might not know
B) Because the seller might change his mind
C) So the judge can identify the sold item from testimony
D) Because it is a Sunnah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, any exception to a sale must be explicitly specified in the contract; mutual knowledge does not remove the obligation.

Invalidity of Claiming Usufruct by Boundaries
Ruling on invalid claim of usufruct by boundaries, with qiyas and application to upper/lower floor sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a claim of usufruct (sukna) by specifying boundaries is invalid and apply the Hanafi rule that usufruct is identified by the land when permanently attached.

Core Idea
Usufruct is movable, cannot be bounded; when attached, it follows land boundaries.

This lesson examines a peculiar issue in Hanafi property law: how to validly claim a usufruct right (sukna). The source passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that usufruct is considered a movable right, so it cannot be identified by boundaries like land. However, when usufruct is permanently attached to a building, it takes on the land's definition. We will explore the reasoning behind this rule and apply it to the sale of upper and lower floors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage begins by stating that a claim of usufruct (sukna) by specifying boundaries is not valid. Understand that the ruling is about the method of identifying the usufruct in a claim.
This is the primary hukm: the claim is invalid if boundaries alone are given.
2
Understand the legal nature of usufruct
The text explains that usufruct is movable (naqli). In Hanafi fiqh, movables are not defined by boundaries; pointing to them suffices. Grasp the difference between movable and immovable property in identification.
Usufruct is movable because it is a right, not a physical object, but when attached to land it becomes fixed.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The author uses qiyas: other movables are identified by pointing, not boundaries, because they can be brought to court. Usufruct cannot be brought because it is fixed in the building. Trace the illa (cause): impossibility of physical movement.
This analogy justifies treating usufruct like land for identification purposes.
4
Apply to the upper/lower floor case
The passage discusses buying an upper floor without a lower floor. The ruling: define the lower floor, not the upper, because the lower is the base and the upper is dependent. Note the exception: if the upper has a separate room, then define the upper.
This follows the principle that defining the origin suffices for the dependent.
5
Derive the mufta bihi position
The operative position is that a claim of usufruct by boundaries alone is invalid. For a valid claim, one must either point to the property (if movable) or refer to the land if the usufruct is attached. Also, when selling units, defining the lower floor suffices for the upper unless the upper is a separate entity.
This is the relied-upon fatwa from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : ادعى سكنى دار و نحوه وبين حدوده لا يصح إذ السكنى نقلي فلا يحد بشيء، وإن كان السكنى نقلياً لكن لما اتصل بالأرض اتصال تأبيد كان تعريفه بما به تعريف الأرض إذ في سائر النقليات إنما لا يعرف بالحدود لإمكان إحضاره فيستغني بالإشارة إليه عن الحد أما السكنى فنقله لا يمكن لأنه مركب في البناء تركيب قرار فالتحق بما لا يمكن نقله أصلا شرى علو بيت ليس له سفل يحد السفل لا العلو إذ السفل مبيع من وجه من حيث إن قرار العلو عليه فلا بد من تحديده وتحديده يغني عن تحديد العلو إذ العلو عرف بتحديد السفل ولأن السفل أصل والعلو تبع فتحديد الأصل أولى. هذا إذا لم يكن حول العلو حجرة، فلو كانت ينبغي أن يحد العلو لأنه المبيع فلا بد من إعلامه وهو بحده وقد أمكن ا هـ .
Then he said: He claimed the usufruct of a house and the like and specified its boundaries; it is not valid because usufruct is movable, so it is not bounded by anything. Even though usufruct is movable, since it is permanently attached to the land, its definition is by what defines the land. For other movables, they are not known by boundaries because they can be brought, so pointing to them suffices instead of boundaries. As for usufruct, it cannot be moved because it is fixed in the building as a fixture, so it becomes like something that cannot be moved at all. He bought an upper floor without a lower floor; he should define the lower floor, not the upper, because the lower floor is sold in a way since the upper rests on it, so defining it is necessary and defining it suffices for the upper because the upper is known by defining the lower, and because the lower is the origin and the upper is dependent, so defining the origin is better. This is if there is no room around the upper; if there is, then the upper should be defined because it is the sold item, so it must be notified by its boundary, and that is possible. Ends.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage addresses claims related to usufruct (sukna), which is a right to use a property. The text explains that usufruct is considered a movable (manqul) right, but when it is attached to land permanently (such as the right to live in a house), it is identified by the land's boundaries. However, a claim of usufruct cannot be made merely by specifying boundaries because the usufruct itself is not physically bounded. The discussion then shifts to a specific case: buying an upper floor (ulu) without a lower floor (sifl). The ruling is that one should define the lower floor, not the upper, because the lower is the base and the upper is dependent on it. However, if the upper floor has its own separate room around it, then the upper must be defined because it is the sold item and its boundary can be determined. This shows the principle that when a thing is dependent on another, defining the original suffices.

Key Terms
السكنى (al-sukna): Usufruct; the right to live in a property.
نقلي (naqli): Movable; something that can be moved.
علو ('ulu): Upper floor of a building.
سفل (sufl): Lower floor of a building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a claim of usufruct (sukna) by specifying boundaries not valid?
A) Because usufruct is immovable
B) Because usufruct is movable and cannot be bounded
C) Because boundaries are only for land
D) Because the witness is required to see the interior

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, usufruct is a movable right, so it cannot be claimed by boundaries; when attached to land, identification follows the land.

Defining 'Aqar in Hanafi Fiqh
Lexical definitions of 'aqar from two classical dictionaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'aqar (real estate) according to the Hanafi lexicographical tradition, distinguish it from manqul (movables), and state how this classification affects legal rulings such as shuf'a (pre-emption).

Core Idea
‘Aqar is property with a fixed foundation; its definition determines legal rights such as pre-emption.

In Hanafi fiqh, the classification of property as 'aqar (real estate) or manqul (movable) is not merely a lexical curiosity—it directly affects the application of rulings like shuf'a, liability for defects, and inheritance shares. This lesson examines two authoritative lexicons, al-Misbah and al-Mughrib, to establish the precise scope of 'aqar. Understanding these definitions will allow you to correctly identify which properties fall under real estate rulings in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition from al-Misbah
Identify the key phrase 'kullu mulkin thābitin lahu aslun' and the examples given (house and palm trees). Note the primary meaning: immovable property with a foundation.
Al-Misbah also mentions a secondary, broader usage that includes goods (mata').
2
Extract the definition from al-Mughrib
Identify the phrase 'al-'aqar huwa al-day'a' and the alternative description 'kullu mālin lahu aslun'. Compare with al-Misbah: note that al-Mughrib emphasises 'estate' (day'a) but also includes same essential concept of fixed foundation.
Both sources agree on the essential characteristic: thubūt (fixedness) and aṣl (foundation).
3
Identify the scope of 'aqar in Hanafi fiqh
From the definitions, infer that 'aqar includes houses, land, and palm trees (and by extension other trees). It may also include buildings and structures. The key element is immovability and permanent attachment to land.
Hanafi jurists treat trees as part of the land until severed, so they are 'aqar.
4
Distinguish 'aqar from manqul
Contrast with manqul (movables) such as furniture, animals, and cash. These lack thubūt and are not considered 'aqar. The distinction affects pre-emption, liability for hidden defects, and other sales regulations.
A house is 'aqar; the furniture inside is manqul. Shuf'a applies only to 'aqar.
5
Note the broader usage and its limits
Both lexicons mention that 'aqar is sometimes applied to goods (mata') but this is secondary. In legal contexts, the primary, narrower meaning is operational unless context indicates otherwise.
This broader usage is rare in fiqh texts; default is the foundational meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المصباح: العقار كسلام كل ملك ثابت له أصل كالدار والنخل، وربما أطلق على المتاع والجمع عقارات ا هـ . . وفي المغرب : العقار الضيعة وقيل كل مال له أصل كالدار والضيعة ا هـ .
In al-Misbah: Al-'aqar (like salam) is every fixed property that has a foundation, like a house and palm trees, and sometimes it is applied to goods (mata'), and the plural is aqarat. Ends. And in al-Mughrib: Al-'aqar is an estate (day'a), and it is said every property that has a foundation, like a house and an estate. Ends.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides two lexical definitions of the term 'aqar' (real estate) from classical Arabic dictionaries. In al-Misbah, 'aqar is defined as any property with a fixed foundation, such as a house or palm trees. It can also be broadly used for movable goods, though the primary meaning is immovable. In al-Mughrib, the definition is more specific: an estate (day'a) or any property with a foundation. These definitions are important in Hanafi fiqh because the classification of property as 'aqar or manqul affects rulings on sales, claims, and shuf'a (pre-emption).

Key Terms
العقار (al-'aqar): Real estate: property with a fixed foundation like houses and palm trees.
المتاع (al-mata'): Goods, movables.
الضيعة (al-day'a): Estate, agricultural land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definitions cited in the passage, which of the following is NOT an example of 'aqar?
A) A house (dār)
B) A palm tree (nakhl)
C) An estate (day'a)
D) A carpet (bisāṭ)

Key Takeaway
‘Aqar is defined in Hanafi fiqh as immovable property with a fixed foundation, including land, buildings, and trees, and this classification governs rights such as pre-emption.

Trees and Buildings as Movable Property for Pre-emption
Ruling on classification of trees and buildings as movables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether trees and buildings are subject to pre-emption when sold without land, and explain the reason based on their classification as movable property.

Core Idea
For pre-emption, buildings and trees are movable goods — no shuf'a without land.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, Ibn Nujaym addresses a critical classification issue in shuf'a (pre-emption): are buildings and date palms immovable (aqar) or movable (manqul)? The ruling has direct practical consequences because shuf'a only applies to immovable property. The author affirms the established Hanafi position that they are movables, and firmly rejects a contemporary fatwa that treated date palms as real estate. You will also see a secondary ruling about claims for price and the need to produce the sold item in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Find the statement where the mashayikh explicitly classify buildings and date palms. Note that this classification determines whether shuf'a applies.
The ruling is 'لا شفعة فيهما إذا بيعا بلا عرصة' — no pre-emption if sold without land.
2
Understand the exception
When buildings or trees are sold with the land (عرصة), shuf'a applies as a subsidiary right (تبعاً). This shows the classification is not absolute but context-dependent.
3
Analyze the contemporary error
Ibn Nujaym criticizes a contemporary who misclassified date palms as real estate (عقار). Contrast this with the correct position: they are manqulat (movable).
The author says he was warned but did not recant – highlighting the danger of issuing fatwa against established madhhab positions.
4
Examine the procedural sub-rule
The passage mentions a rule from Jami' al-Fusulayn: if a claim is for the price of an undelivered good, the good must be brought to court; if delivered, no need. This distinguishes a pure debt claim from a sale claim.
This sub-rule is related to proof of sale, not directly to shuf'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد صرح مشايخنا في كتاب الشفعة بأن البناء والنخل من المنقولات وأنه لا شفعة فيهما إذا بيعا بلا عرصة فإن بيعا معها وجبت تبعاً وسيأتي بيانه إن شاء الله تعالى فيها. وقد غلط بعض العصريين فجعل النخيل من العقار وأفتى به ونبه فلم يرجع كعادته. وقيد بدعوى المحدود إذ لو ادعى ثمن محدود لم يشترط بيان حدوده؛ كذا في السراجية. وفي جامع الفصولين: ولو ادعى ثمن مبيع لم يقبض لا بد من إحضار المبيع مجلس الحكم حتى يثبت البيع عند القاضي بخلاف ما لو ادعى ثمن مبيع قبض فإنه لا يجب إحضاره لأنه دعوى الدين حقيقة ا هـ .
And our scholars have stated explicitly in the Book of Pre-emption that buildings and date palms are among movable properties and that there is no pre-emption in them if sold without land; if sold with land, pre-emption becomes obligatory as a subsidiary. And its explanation will come later, God willing. And some contemporaries have erred by making date palms real estate and issued a fatwa to that effect, and he was warned but did not recant, as is his habit. And it is restricted to a claim for a bounded [object]; for if one claims a bounded price, specification of its limits is not required; likewise in al-Sirajiyya. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: if one claims the price of a sold item that has not been delivered, it is necessary to bring the sold item to the court chamber so that the sale can be established before the judge, unlike if he claims the price of a sold item that has been delivered, for then it is not necessary to bring it, because it is truly a claim of debt. End.)
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage states two rulings. First, buildings and trees are legally classified as movable property (manqulat) for the purpose of pre-emption (shuf'a). Therefore, if they are sold without land, no right of pre-emption exists; but if they are sold with land, pre-emption becomes a subsidiary right. The author criticizes a contemporary scholar who incorrectly treated date palms as real estate (aqar) and issued a fatwa accordingly, and persisted in his error. The second ruling concerns the condition for a valid claim: if the claim is for a bounded object (mahdud), its limits must be specified. However, if one claims a 'bounded price' (thaman mahdud), no such specification is needed. The passage then distinguishes between a claim for the price of an undelivered good (must produce the good to prove the sale) versus a delivered good (no need, as it is a debt claim).

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption; the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold share to prevent harm.
منقول (manqul): Movable property; can be transported without damage.
عقار (aqar): Immovable property; land and what is permanently attached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells a building without the land, does the pre-emption right apply?
A) Yes, because building is attached to land
B) No, because building is movable property
C) Yes, if the buyer is a neighbor
D) No, unless the building is a mosque

Key Takeaway
Buildings and trees are movable for pre-emption; sold without land, no shuf'a — a ruling based on explicit statements of the Hanafi mashayikh.

When a Plaintiff Has a Witness but Demands an Oath: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath and witness presence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa denies the plaintiff the right to demand an oath when a witness is present, and to distinguish the practical consequences of the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
The right to demand an oath is conditional on inability to present evidence – a present witness bars the oath.

In litigation, a plaintiff may have a witness ready to testify but might prefer to have the defendant swear an oath. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether this is permissible. Understanding this dispute requires examining the underlying principles and the precise conditions under which an oath may be demanded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Procedural Question
The passage addresses: when a plaintiff says, 'I have a present witness (bayyinah ḥāḍirah) but request the defendant's oath,' should the judge administer the oath?
This is a procedural matter in qaḍā' (judicial procedure).
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and His Principle
Abu Hanifa says the judge should NOT administer the oath. His reasoning: the right to an oath is contingent upon being unable to produce evidence (ثبوت الحق في اليمين مرتب على العجز عن إقامة البينة). Since the plaintiff has a witness, he is not unable, so the oath is not his right.
This principle is derived from a narration (ما رويناه).
3
State Abu Yusuf's Position and His Dalil
Abu Yusuf says the judge SHOULD administer the oath because a well-known hadith states that the oath is the plaintiff's right (اليمين حقه بالحديث المعروف). Therefore, if he demands it, he must be answered.
Muhammad's position varies between the two views depending on the source.
4
Note the Qualifications Regarding the Witness's Location
The ruling is qualified: if the witness is present in the court, no oath by consensus; if the witness is in the city but not in court, there is disagreement; if the witness is outside the city (defined as travel distance), oath is allowed by agreement. Also, if the plaintiff has a witness but does not inform the judge, he may choose between oath and evidence.
These qualifications show the nuanced application of the principle.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, the plaintiff must present the witness and cannot demand an oath. Under Abu Yusuf, the plaintiff has the option to present the witness or demand an oath. The difference affects the judge's duty and the plaintiff's strategy.
The text does not give a clear tarjih; later practice may favor flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو قال المدعي لي بينة حاضرة وطلب اليمين لم يستحلف) أي عند أبي حنيفة . وقال أبو يوسف : يستحلف لأن اليمين حقه بالحديث المعروف فإذا طالبه به يجيبه. ولأبي حنيفة أن ثبوت الحق في اليمين مرتب على العجز عن إقامة البينة بما رويناه فلا يكون حقه دونه، ومحمد مع أبي يوسف فيما ذكره الخصاف، ومع أبي حنيفة فيما ذكره الطحاوي. أطلق في حضورها فشمل حضورها في مجلس الحكم ولا خلاف أنه لا يحلف وحضورها في المصر وهو محل الاختلاف وحضورها في المصر بصفة المرض، وظاهر ما في خزانة المفتين خلافه فإنه قال : الاستحلاف يجري في الدعاوى الصحيحة إذا أنكر المدعى عليه ويقول المدعي لا شهود لي أو شهودي غيب أو مرضى اهـ . وقيد بحضورها لأنها لو كانت خارج المصر فإنه يحلف اتفاقاً. وفي المجتبى وقدرت الغيبة بمسيرة السفر .اهـ. وقيد بقوله «المدعي» لأنه لو كان له بينة عادلة حاضرة ولم يخبر القاضي بها فهو مخير بين الاستحلاف وبين إقامة البينة؛ كذا في القنية. ثم رقم بعده لآخر : إن غلب على ظنه أنه ينكل فله أن يحلفه، وإن غلب على ظنه أنه يحلف كاذباً لا يعذر في التحليف وفيها أيضاً ادعى المديون الإيصال فأنكر المدعي ولا بينة له فطلب يمينه فقال المدعي اجعل حقي في الختم ثم استحلفني فله ذلك في زماننا
His statement: (And if the plaintiff says: I have a present witness and requests the oath, he is not sworn) i.e. according to Abu Hanifah. And Abu Yusuf said: He is sworn, because the oath is his right by the well-known hadith, so if he demands it, he is answered. And for Abu Hanifah: The establishment of the right to the oath is conditional on inability to produce evidence, as we have narrated, so it is not his right without that. And Muhammad is with Abu Yusuf in what al-Khassaf mentioned, and with Abu Hanifah in what al-Tahawi mentioned. He (the author) spoke in absolute terms regarding its presence, so it includes its presence in the court of judgment: there is no disagreement that he is not sworn. And its presence in the city: this is the point of disagreement. And its presence in the city in a state of illness. And what appears in Khizanat al-Muftin is contrary, for he said: Administering an oath occurs in valid claims when the defendant denies and the plaintiff says: I have no witnesses, or my witnesses are absent or ill. End. And he restricted it to its presence, because if it were outside the city, then he is sworn by agreement. And in al-Mujtaba, absence is defined as travel distance. End. And he restricted it by his saying 'the plaintiff' because if he had a just witness present and did not inform the judge of it, he has the option between administering the oath and presenting the evidence. Thus in al-Qunyah. Then he added after it: If he thinks it probable that he will refuse the oath, he may have him sworn. And if he thinks it probable that he will swear falsely, he is not excused in having him sworn. And in it also: The debtor claimed payment, and the plaintiff denied and had no evidence, so he requested his oath. The plaintiff said: Put my right in the seal then administer the oath to me, then he may do that in our time.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural question: if a plaintiff has a witness present (ready to testify) but instead requests that the defendant be made to take an oath, should the judge administer the oath? According to Abu Hanifa, the judge should not, because the right to demand an oath is conditional on the inability to produce evidence. Since the plaintiff has a witness, he is not unable. Abu Yusuf disagrees, citing a well-known hadith that an oath is the plaintiff's right. Muhammad is reported to have held both views in different sources. The rule is qualified: if the witness is present in the court, no oath by consensus; if the witness is in the city but not in court, there is disagreement; if the witness is outside the city (defined as travel distance), the oath is allowed by consensus. Additionally, if the plaintiff has a just witness but does not inform the judge, he has the option to present the evidence or demand an oath. The passage also mentions that if the plaintiff believes the defendant will refuse to swear, he may demand the oath; but if he believes the defendant will swear falsely, he is not excused in demanding it. Finally, a practical note: the plaintiff may request that his right be placed in a seal and then swear.

Key Terms
بينة حاضرة (bayyinah ḥāḍirah): A witness who is present and available to testify
العجز عن إقامة البينة (al-ʿajz ʿan iqāmat al-bayyinah): Inability to produce evidence
اليمين حقه (al-yamīn ḥaqquhu): The oath is his (the plaintiff's) right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is a plaintiff not allowed to demand an oath when he has a present witness?
A) Because the witness may be unreliable.
B) Because the right to oath is conditional on inability to produce evidence.
C) Because the defendant has the right to refuse.
D) Because the judge prefers evidence over oath.

Key Takeaway
A present witness defeats the right to an oath according to Abu Hanifa; Abu Yusuf allows the plaintiff to choose, and later practice often adopts this flexibility.

Oath in Sale Claims: Payment vs No Payment
Clarifies oath wording in sale claims from Khizanat al-Muftin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a claim of purchase with payment (ownership claim) and without (contract claim), and state the correct oath wording for each scenario.

Core Idea
Mention of payment transforms the claim into ownership, changing the oath.

In Hanafi litigation, the wording of the oath in sale claims depends on whether the buyer alleges that he paid the price. This distinction, detailed in al-Khizanah, affects the legal nature of the claim and the proper oath to be administered. Understanding this nuance prevents procedural errors in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Determine whether the buyer mentioned payment of the price (nagd al-thaman) or not.
If payment is mentioned, the claim is about ownership; if not, it is about the contract.
2
Classify the legal nature of the claim
A claim with payment is a claim of absolute ownership (da'wa milk), not a claim of contract. A claim without payment is a claim of contract (da'wa 'aqd) and requires the price to be produced.
Ownership claims can be valid even if the price is unknown; contract claims require specification.
3
Determine the correct oath wording
For ownership claim: defendant swears that the sold item is not the claimant's property nor owed. For contract claim (after price is produced): defendant swears no obligation to receive price and deliver item, or no current purchase exists.
The oath focuses on ownership, not on the act of sale.
4
Apply to hypothetical cases
Practice by taking sample claims and drafting the appropriate oath.
This ensures procedural accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن ما ذكره في حلف البيع قاصر والحق ما في الخزانة من التفصيل فإن المشتري إذا ادعى الشراء، فإن ذكر نقد الثمن فادعى عليه يحلف بالله ما هذا العبد ملك المدعي ولا ء منه بالسبب الذي ادعى ولا يخلف بالله ما بعته وإن لم يذكر المشتري نقد الثمن يقال له أحضر الثمن فإذا حضر استحلفه القاضي بالله ما عليك قبض هذا الثمن وتسليم هذا العبد من الوجه الذي ادعى وإن شاء حلف بالله ما بينك وبين هذا شراء قائم الساعة. والحاصل دعوى الشراء مع نقد الثمن دعوى المبيع ملكاً مطلقاً وليست بدعوى العقد ولهذا تصح مع جهالة الثمن فيحلف على ملك المبيع، ودعوى البيع مع تسليم المبيع دعوى الثمن معنى وليست بدعوى العقد ولهذا تصح مع جهالة المبيع فيحلف على ملك الثمن اهـ.
And among them [the examples] is that what he mentioned about the oath of sale is insufficient, and the truth is the detail in al-Khizanah: if the buyer claims purchase, if he mentions the payment of the price, then he is sued, he swears: 'By Allah, this slave is not the property of the claimant, nor [does he owe] any part of it by the cause he claims,' and he does not change to swearing 'I did not sell it.' If the buyer does not mention the payment of the price, he is told: 'Bring the price.' When he brings it, the judge administers an oath: 'By Allah, you have no obligation to receive this price and deliver this slave from the aspect he claims,' or if he wishes, he swears: 'By Allah, there is no current purchase between you and this [person] at this moment.' The conclusion: the claim of purchase with mention of payment of the price is a claim of absolute ownership of the sold item, not a claim of contract, therefore it is valid even with ignorance of the price, so he swears on ownership of the sold item. The claim of sale with delivery of the sold item is essentially a claim of the price, not a claim of contract, therefore it is valid even with ignorance of the sold item, so he swears on ownership of the price. End.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on the wording of oaths in sale claims, correcting a previous insufficient statement. The author distinguishes two scenarios: (1) If the buyer claims purchase and mentions that he paid the price, the claim is essentially one of ownership over the sold item. The defendant (seller) swears that the claimant does not own the item, nor does the defendant owe anything from that cause. (2) If the buyer does not mention payment, he must first produce the price. Then the judge administers an oath that the defendant has no obligation to receive the price and deliver the item, or the defendant can swear that there is no valid purchase between them. The key principle is that claiming purchase with payment is a claim of ownership (of the sold item), while claiming sale with delivery is a claim of ownership (of the price). Hence the oath focuses on the respective ownership.

Key Terms
نقد الثمن (naqd al-thaman): The payment of the price in cash, i.e., the buyer alleges he paid the price.
دعوى العقد (da‘wā al-‘aqd): A claim specifically about the contract itself, not about absolute ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Bring the sold item
B) Produce the price (thaman)
C) Swear immediately
D) Bring witnesses

Key Takeaway
The wording of the oath in sale claims depends on whether the buyer mentions payment; mentioning payment shifts the claim from contract to ownership.

Resolving Disputes on Price or Goods: Evidence and Mutual Oaths
Detailed rules on evidence and mutual oaths in sale disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for resolving disputes between buyer and seller regarding the amount of price or goods, distinguishing between cases with and without evidence.

Core Idea
When buyer and seller differ, evidence prevails; if both have evidence, the higher claim wins; if none, mutual oaths rescind the sale.

Disputes over the exact price or goods in a sale are common. This lesson explains how a Hanafi qadi resolves such disputes: first by looking at evidence (bayyinah), then by mutual oaths (tahaluf) if evidence is lacking, and finally by preferring the party who proves a higher amount or a more specific fact. The rulings are derived from the principles of evidence and the need to avoid contradiction in claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
Determine whether the buyer and seller differ on the price (e.g., amount, type, description) or the sold item (e.g., identity, condition). The ruling hinges on the type of difference and the availability of evidence.
2
Check for Evidence (Bayyinah)
If one party brings valid evidence (usually two male witnesses or one male and two females), judgment is given for that party because evidence outweighs a bare claim.
Bayyinah is defined as clear proof; its strength over mere claim is a core Hanafi principle.
3
Apply the Rule for Increase (Ziyadah)
If both parties present evidence, the party that proves an increase (e.g., a higher price or more goods) wins. This is because evidence is for proof, and establishing a higher value does not contradict the other's evidence—it merely shows additional fact.
For example, seller claims price 1000, buyer says 800; seller's evidence of 1000 wins because proving more does not negate the buyer's claim.
4
Apply Mutual Oaths (Tahaluf) when Evidence Absent
If neither party has evidence, they take mutual oaths. The buyer swears that he did not agree to the seller's claim, and the seller swears similarly. If both swear, the contract is rescinded (faskh). If one refuses to swear, the other's claim is upheld.
Mutual oaths are wajib; they apply not only to price but also to genus, type, description, and capital in salam.
5
Consider Evidence Preference on Price vs. Goods
When the parties differ on both price and goods, the seller's evidence is preferred on price (since he is the one demanding payment), and the buyer's evidence is preferred on the goods (since he is the one receiving the item). This ensures each party's evidence is applied where it is most specific.
This is illustrated by the example of a slave-girl sold for a slave vs. 100 dinars; the seller's evidence is taken on the price (the slave), the buyer's on the goods (the slave-girl).

Source Text

The author indicated that if they differ on the price and the sold item, the seller's evidence is preferred regarding the price, and the buyer's evidence is preferred regarding the sold item, considering the increase in proof. If they dropped the amount, it would be better because the difference in the description of the price and genus is similar, as in al-Hidayah in explaining difference in term, and it will be elaborated later. They differed on the genus of the price and both presented evidence; the evidence is of the one with whom there is no agreement. Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence or proof, typically two witnesses.
الزيادة (al-ziyādah): The increase claimed by one party over what the other acknowledges.
التحالف (al-tahāluf): Mutual oaths taken by both parties when they differ and have no evidence.

Burden of Proof in Contract Validity Disputes
Procedural ruling with intra-Hanafi nuance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default rule for who bears the burden of proof when one party claims a contract is valid and the other claims it is invalid, and identify the exception for core defects in non-Zahir al-Riwayah.

Core Idea
In a contract dispute, the one claiming validity holds the statement, unless a core defect is alleged in non-Zahir al-Riwayah.

In Hanafi fiqh, when two parties dispute the validity of a contract, the burden of proof follows a clear rule: the one who claims validity has the statement, and the one who claims invalidity must produce evidence. However, there is a lesser-known report from Abu Hanifa that if the invalidity claim involves a defect in the core of the contract, the burden shifts. This lesson unpacks the default principle, the nuance, and how later scholars reconciled conflicting transmissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Read the first sentence: 'the statement is for the one claiming validity, and the evidence is for the one claiming invalidity, by agreement.' This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
Note the term 'statement' (al-qawl) refers to the right to speak first without producing evidence; the burden of proof is on the other party.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi exception
The text mentions a non-Zahir al-Riwayah report: 'whoever claims a defect in the core of the contract, the statement is for him.' This means if the invalidity claim attacks the essence (e.g., missing pillar), the claimant of invalidity gets the statement.
This is an alternative transmission (riwayah) not from the six core books, so it carries less weight in practice.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author (al-Bahr al-Raiq) questions a passage in al-Bazzaziyyah that says for voidness (butlan) the statement goes to the claimant of voidness. He argues this conflicts with the non-Zahir al-Riwayah principle, which limits the exception to core defects.
This shows how later Hanafi scholars critically examine earlier works to ensure consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتبايعان أحدهما يدعي الصحة والآخر الفساد فالقول لمدعي الصحة والبيئة لمدعي الفساد وفاقاً. وفي غير ظاهر الرواية عن أبي حنيفة من ادعى فساداً في صلب العقد فالقول له. ثم نقل عن الأشباه : اخلف المتبايعان في الصحة والفساد فالقول لمدعي الصحة؛ كذا في الخانية. ولو اختلفا في والبطلان فالقول لمدعي البطلان؟ كذا في البزازية ثم قال : يقول الحقير ما في البزازية محل نظر لما مر أن في غير ظاهر الرواية لو ادعى فساداً في صلب العقد فالقول له اهـ. ذكر هذا في بحث اختلاف المتبايعين من الفصل ۲۹.
The two transacting parties: one claims validity and the other invalidity; then the statement is for the one claiming validity, and the evidence is for the one claiming invalidity, by agreement. In non-Zahir al-Riwayah from Abu Hanifa: whoever claims a defect in the core of the contract, the statement is for him. Then he reported from al-Ashbah: If the two transacting parties differ on validity and invalidity, the statement is for the one claiming validity; similarly in al-Khaniyyah. If they differ on voidness, the statement is for the one claiming voidness? Similarly in al-Bazzaziyyah. Then he said: The humble [author] says: What is in al-Bazzaziyyah is questionable because of what was mentioned that in non-Zahir al-Riwayah, if someone claims a defect in the core of the contract, the statement is for him. This is mentioned in the discussion of the difference between transacting parties in chapter 29.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This lesson presents the rule when one party claims a contract is valid (sahih) and the other claims it is invalid (fasid) or void (batil). The established rule is that the one claiming validity has the statement (al-qawl) and the one claiming invalidity must produce evidence. This is agreed upon. However, there is a non-Zahir al-Riwayah report from Abu Hanifa that if someone claims a defect in the core of the contract, the statement is for him (i.e., the one claiming invalidity). The author notes a contradiction in al-Bazzaziyyah regarding voidness and resolves it by preferring the non-Zahir al-Riwayah report that the one claiming a core defect has the statement.

Key Terms
الصحة (al-sihhah): Validity of a contract according to Islamic law.
الفساد (al-fasād): Invalidity or defectiveness of a contract; a fasid contract is one that lacks a necessary condition but can be corrected.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Voidness; a contract that is fundamentally invalid and cannot be rectified.
القول (al-qawl): The statement; the right to speak first without producing evidence, shifting the burden to the opponent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The default rule places the burden on the claimant of invalidity, except when a core defect is alleged in non-Zahir al-Riwayah.

6 min
Evidence Prevails Over Bare Claim in Sale Disputes
Explains the ruling when buyer and seller differ on price or object and one has evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that when buyer and seller dispute the price or object, judgment goes to the party who provides evidence, and apply it to cases involving tahaluf and multiple evidence.

Core Idea
Evidence (bayyinah) outweighs a mere claim in sale disputes; the one who proves wins.

When buyer and seller disagree on the price or the exact item sold, how does Islamic law resolve it? This lesson from the Hanafi school gives a clear rule: whoever brings evidence wins. But what happens when neither has evidence, or both do? The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks these scenarios, including the role of mutual oaths (tahaluf) and how to prioritize evidence when both sides provide proof.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The matn says: if they differ in the amount of the price or the sold item, judgment is given to the one who provides proof. This is because evidence is stronger than a bare claim.
2
Examine the scope
The author notes that 'price' includes capital in salam contracts, and the same rule applies to differences in genus, type, description, or amount.
3
Apply the no-evidence scenario
When neither has evidence, they take mutual oaths (tahaluf) and the contract is rescinded. See the slave-with-cut example from al-Kafi.
Tahaluf results in contract rescission.
4
Handle the both-have-evidence scenario
If both bring evidence, the seller's evidence is preferred for the price (he affirms a higher amount), and the buyer's evidence is preferred for the sold item (he affirms a larger object). This is because evidence is for affirmation, and the party affirming more gets priority.
No conflict: each evidence addresses a different element.
5
Consider related differences
The same principle applies to differences in description or genus of the sold item.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (اختلفا في قدر الثمن أو المبيع قضى لمن برهن) أي اختلف البائع والمشتري في قدر أحدهما وأقام أحدهما بينة قضي له لأن في الجانب الآخر مجرد الدعوى والبينة أقوى منها. وفي المصباح : البرهان الحجة وإيضاحها قيل النون زائدة وقيل أصلية، وحكى الأزهري القولين فقال في باب الثلاثي النون زائدة. وقوله برهن فلان مولد والصواب أن يقال أبره إذا جاء بالبرهان كما قال ابن الأعرابي وقال في باب الرباعي برهن إذا أتى بحجته .اهـ اعلم أنه يدخل في الثمن رأس المال في المبيع المسلم فيه وقدمنا في بابه أنهما يتحالفان إذا اختلفا في جنسه أو نوعه أو صفته أو قدر رأس المال أو المسلم فيه ويتحالفان ويفسخ السلم، ويدخل أيضاً ما في الكافي : عبد قطع عند البائع فقال البائع قطعه المشتري قبل البيع ولي عليه نصف القيمة وكل الثمن وقال المشتري قطعه البائع بعد البيع ولي أخذه بنصف الثمن أو تركه ولا بينة تحالفا، فإن حلفا أخذه المشتري بكل ثمنه أو ترك، وإن برهنا فلمشتريه، وإن اتفقا إن قاطعه بائعه أو مشتريه أو أجنبي وادعاه البائع قبل البيع والمشتري بعده فالقول والبينة لمشتريه .اهـ. ويدخل في الاختلاف في المبيع ما في الكافي : ادعى أنه باعه هذا العبد بمائة دينار وقال المشتري ما اشتريت إلا نصفه بخمسمائة درهم فالقول لمشتريه في النصف وتحالفا في النصف فيحلف المشتري في النصفين يميناً واحدة، فإن نكل لزمه البيع بمائة دينار، وإن حلف لم يثبت البيع في أحد النصفين ويحلف بائعه، فإن نكل لزمه البيع بخمسمائة وإن حلف فسخ البيع وتمامه فيه قوله : (وإن برهنا فللمثبت الزيادة) لأن البينات للإثبات ولا تعارض في الزيادة. وأشار المؤلف إلى أنهما لو اختلفا في الثمن والمبيع فبينة البائع أولى في الثمن، وبينة المشتري أولى في المبيع نظراً إلى زيادة الإثبات، ولو حذف القدر لكان أولى لأن الاختلاف في وصف الثمن والجنس كذلك كما في الهداية في بيان الاختلاف في الأجل وسيأتي له مزيد .اهـ.
His statement: (They differ in the amount of the price or the sold item, judgment is given to the one who provides proof) meaning the seller and buyer differ in the amount of one of them and one of them establishes evidence, judgment is given to him because on the other side is merely a claim and evidence is stronger than it. In al-Misbah: al-burhan is proof and its clarification; it is said the nun is extra and it is said it is original, and al-Azhari narrated the two opinions and said in the chapter on triliteral verbs the nun is extra. And his statement 'barhana fulan' is a neologism, and the correct thing is to say 'abrah' if he comes with proof as Ibn al-A'rabi said, and in the chapter on quadriliteral verbs 'barhana' if he presents his evidence. End. Know that enters into the price the capital in the sold item in salam, and we have previously presented in its chapter that they take mutual oaths if they differ in its genus, type, description, or amount of capital or the salam commodity, and they take mutual oaths and the salam is annulled. Also enters what is in al-Kafi: a slave was cut by the seller, the seller said the buyer cut him before the sale and I have half the value and all the price, and the buyer said the seller cut him after the sale and I have the option to take him for half the price or leave him, and there is no evidence; they take mutual oaths. If they both swear, the buyer takes him at the full price or leaves. If they both provide proof, then it is for the buyer. If they agree that his cutter was the seller or the buyer or a stranger, and the seller claims it before the sale and the buyer after, then the statement and evidence are for the buyer. End. Also enters into the difference regarding the sold item what is in al-Kafi: he claimed that he sold him this slave for one hundred dinars and the buyer said I only bought half for five hundred dirhams; the statement is for the buyer regarding the half, and they take mutual oaths regarding the half; the buyer swears one oath for both halves; if he refuses, the sale is binding for one hundred dinars; if he swears, the sale is not established for one of the halves, and the seller swears; if he refuses, the sale is binding for five hundred; if he swears, the sale is annulled. And its completion in it is his statement: (And if they both provide proof, then for the one who affirms the increase) because evidences are for affirmation and there is no conflict regarding the increase. And the author indicated that if they differ in the price and the sold item, then the seller's evidence is preferred regarding the price, and the buyer's evidence is preferred regarding the sold item, looking at the increase in affirmation. And if he omitted the amount, it would be better, because the difference in the description of the price and the genus is similar, as in al-Hidayah in the explanation of difference regarding the term, and more will come. End.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, explaining the ruling when buyer and seller dispute the amount of the price or the sold item. The author begins by stating that if one party provides evidence (bayyinah), judgment is given to that party, because a claim without evidence is weaker. He then discusses the etymology of 'burhan' (proof). Following this, he explains that the ruling applies also to the capital in salam sales. Several illustrative cases from al-Kafi are presented: one involving a slave with a cut, and another involving a dispute over the quantity of a slave sold. In both, the rules of tahaluf (mutual oaths) apply when there is no evidence, and if evidence is provided, it takes precedence. The author further notes that if both parties bring evidence, the seller's evidence is preferred regarding the price, and the buyer's regarding the sold item, because there is more to affirm (the seller affirms a higher price, the buyer affirms a larger object). Finally, he notes that this applies similarly to differences in description and genus.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or proof presented in court, typically witnesses or documents, that establishes a claim.
برهان (burhan): Proof or evidence; a clear argument that establishes a fact.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oaths taken by two disputing parties when neither has evidence and they refuse to accept the other's claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if both buyer and seller provide evidence regarding the price, whose evidence is preferred?
A) The buyer's evidence, because he is the defendant.
B) The seller's evidence, because he affirms a higher price (increase).
C) Neither, the contract is rescinded.
D) The evidence of the one who swore first.

Key Takeaway
In a sale dispute, the party with evidence wins; if both have evidence, the one affirming more gets priority for that element.

Ruling on Conflict of Evidence Regarding the Type of Price
Ruling on conflicting evidence of price type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule when buyer and seller differ on the type of price and both present evidence, and identify whose evidence is preferred.

Core Idea
When both parties have evidence on the price type, the seller's evidence prevails in Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in sales contracts: the buyer and seller disagree about what was exchanged as the price. When both produce witnesses, the Hanafi school applies a specific rule to resolve the conflict. Understanding this rule is essential for adjudicating disputes in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What is the disagreement? The buyer and seller differ on the type (jins) of the price. For example, the seller says the price was a slave, the buyer says it was dinars.
2
Recognize the evidence
Both parties have established evidence (bainah). The rule is not to cancel evidence but to prefer one.
Evidence here means credible witnesses.
3
Apply the rule
The evidence of the one upon whose statement there is no prior agreement is preferred. In the example, the seller's statement that the price was a slave (not cash) is preferred because there is no prior agreement on the type.
The phrase 'من لا اتفاق على قوله' means 'the one upon whose statement there is no agreement'.
4
Note the source
The ruling is recorded in al-Nihayah, a key Hanafi text. This indicates it is a relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلفا في جنس الثمن وأقاما البينة فالبينة بينة من لا اتفاق على قوله فلو قال البائع بعتك هذه الجارية بعبدك هذا وقال المشتري اشتريتها منك بمائة دينار وأقاما البينة فبينة البائع أولى؛ كذا في النهاية .
They differ in the genus of the price and both establish evidence, then the evidence is the evidence of the one upon whose statement there is no agreement. For example, if the seller says, 'I sold you this slave-girl for this slave of yours,' and the buyer says, 'I bought her from you for one hundred dinars,' and both establish evidence, then the seller's evidence is preferred; thus in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This is a short ruling addressing a specific scenario: the buyer and seller disagree about the type (jins) of the price, and both have evidence. The rule is that the evidence of the party whose statement does not align with an agreement is given priority. In the example, the seller says the price was a slave (a specific item), while the buyer says it was cash (dinars). Since there is no prior agreement on the type, and both bring evidence, the seller's evidence is preferred. This is the ruling as recorded in al-Nihayah.

Key Terms
جنس الثمن (jins al-thaman): The category or type of the price, e.g., currency, goods, or services.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A seller claims the price was a slave, the buyer claims it was 100 dinars, and both bring witnesses. According to the passage, whose evidence is preferred?
A) The buyer's evidence is preferred.
B) The seller's evidence is preferred.
C) The evidence of the one who first presented it.
D) The contract is voided and both are discharged.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when buyer and seller disagree on the type of price and both have witnesses, the seller's evidence is preferred.

Dispute on Validity of Sale: Who Bears the Burden of Proof?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on burden of proof in sale validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on burden of proof in sale validity disputes and distinguish the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah position from the non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah exception for core defects.

Core Idea
The one claiming validity is believed; the one claiming a core defect is believed only in non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

When a buyer and seller disagree on whether the sale was valid or invalid, who is believed without evidence? The default ruling in the Hanafi school is that validity is presumed, so the one asserting invalidity must prove it. But a lesser-known narration from Abu Hanifa flips this rule for defects in the core of the contract. This lesson unpacks that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
In any dispute over sale validity, one party claims the contract is valid, the other claims it is invalid (fasād) or void (bāṭil).
The dispute may involve a defect in the core of the contract (e.g., lack of ownership) or a subsidiary issue.
2
State the agreed ruling (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah)
The well-known transmission (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah) holds that the one claiming validity has the statement (al-qawl), meaning his claim is accepted without evidence. The one claiming invalidity must bring proof (al-bayyinah).
This is the general rule and the basis for fatwa.
3
Note the non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah exception
A less authoritative narration from Abu Hanifa states that if someone claims invalidity due to a defect in the core of the contract (fasād fī ṣulb al-ʿaqd), then his statement is accepted without evidence.
This exception applies only to core defects, not minor issues.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The commentator in al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq notes that the ruling from al-Bazzāziyyah (giving statement to claimant of nullity) contradicts the non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah position and is therefore subject to consideration. The preferred view is that the non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah applies for core defects.
The general rule remains unless the defect is core and the claimant lacks evidence.
5
Apply the rule
Given a scenario, determine which party has the burden of proof based on whether the claimed defect is in the core of the contract or not.
Consider the strength of the narration and the availability of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتبايعان أحدهما يدعي الصحة والآخر الفساد فالقول لمدعي الصحة والبيئة لمدعي الفساد وفاقاً. وفي غير ظاهر الرواية عن أبي حنيفة من ادعى فساداً في صلب العقد فالقول له. ثم نقل عن الأشباه : اخلف المتبايعان في الصحة والفساد فالقول لمدعي الصحة؛ كذا في الخانية. ولو اختلفا في والبطلان فالقول لمدعي البطلان؟ كذا في البزازية ثم قال : يقول الحقير ما في البزازية محل نظر لما مر أن في غير ظاهر الرواية لو ادعى فساداً في صلب العقد فالقول له اهـ. ذكر هذا في بحث اختلاف المتبايعين من الفصل ۲۹.
The two contracting parties: one claims validity and the other claims invalidity; the statement is for the one claiming validity, and the evidence is for the one claiming invalidity, by agreement. And in non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah from Abū Ḥanīfah: whoever claims invalidity in the core of the contract, the statement is for him. Then he narrated from al-Ashbāh: the two contracting parties differ in validity and invalidity; the statement is for the one claiming validity; thus in al-Khāniyyah. And if they differ in [؟] and nullity, the statement is for the one claiming nullity? Thus in al-Bazzāziyyah. Then he said: the humble one says: what is in al-Bazzāziyyah is subject to consideration, because as has passed: in non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, if one claims invalidity in the core of the contract, the statement is for him. End. This was mentioned in the discussion of difference between the two contracting parties from chapter 29.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage addresses the dispute between buyer and seller over the validity (ṣiḥḥa) or invalidity (fasād) of the sale contract. The agreed-upon ruling (وفاقاً) is that the one claiming validity has the statement (al-qawl), meaning his claim is accepted without evidence, while the one claiming invalidity must provide proof. However, a non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah narration from Abū Ḥanīfah states that if someone claims a defect in the core of the contract (fasād fī ṣulb al-ʿaqd), the statement is for him. The author then notes a contradiction from al-Ashbāh and al-Khāniyyah that maintain the first ruling, and al-Bazzāziyyah that says the one claiming nullity has the statement. The commentator (the author of al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq) resolves this by noting that al-Bazzāziyyah's statement is questionable because it contradicts the non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah position. Thus, the preferred view is that in non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, the one claiming a core defect has the statement.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The well-known and authoritative transmission within the Hanafi school, primarily from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
صلب العقد (Ṣulb al-ʿAqd): The core or essence of the contract; defects that strike at the validity of the contract itself (e.g., lack of capacity, coercion, illegality).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute where the seller claims the sale is valid and the buyer claims it is invalid due to a defect in the core of the contract, what is the ruling according to the non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah from Abu Hanifa?
A) The seller's claim is accepted without evidence.
B) The buyer's claim is accepted without evidence.
C) Both must provide evidence.
D) The contract is automatically rescinded.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule on burden of proof in sale disputes has a nuanced exception for core defects, known from a non-Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah narration.

The Core Ruling of Mutual Oaths (Tahaluf)
Ruling with proof on mutual oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutual oaths in sale disputes and identify the hadith proof used.

Core Idea
Mutual oaths are wajib when both parties lack evidence, based on the hadith 'تحالفا وترادا'.

This lesson covers the fundamental ruling of mutual oaths (tahaluf) when a buyer and seller disagree and neither can produce evidence. The author explains the legal basis: before possession it is qiyas, after possession it is istihsan. The primary proof is the prophetic hadith commanding mutual oaths and rescission. We will walk through the ruling, its rationale, and the judge's role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Both parties are unable to provide evidence and do not consent to each other's claim. This triggers the need for mutual oaths.
The condition of inability and lack of consent is essential.
2
Distinguish before vs. after possession
Before possession, the ruling is based on qiyas (each is a denier). After possession, it is based on istihsan because the buyer has possession and only the seller claims an increase.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh uses different reasoning methods.
3
Recite the hadith proof
The hadith states: 'If the two contracting parties differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return the purchase.' This is the primary dalil.
The imperative form indicates obligation (wujub).
4
Understand the judge's role
The judge administers an oath to each party regarding the other's claim. He also proposes rescission to encourage settlement.
The judge does not decide the truth; he facilitates the resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن عجزا ولم يرضيا بدعوى أحدهما تحالفا أي استحلف الحاكم كل واحد منهما على دعوى صاحبه، فإن كان قبل القبض فهو قياسي لأن كلاً منهما منكر، وأما بعده فاستحساني فقط لأن المشتري لا يدعي شيئاً لأن المبيع سالم له بقي دعوى البائع في زيادة الثمن والمشتري ينكره فيكتفي بحلفه لكنا عرفناه بالنص وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إذا اختلف المتبايعان والسلعة قائمة بعينها تحالفا وترادا قيد بعدم رضاهما للإشارة إلى أن القاضي يقول لكل منهما إما أن ترضى بدعوى صاحبك وإلا فسخناه لأن المقصود قطع المنازعة وهذا جهة فيه لأنه ربما لا يرضيان بالفسخ فإذا علما به يتراضيان. ولو قال ولم يرض واحد منهما بدعوى صاحبه بدل قوله ولم يرضيا لكان أولى لأن شرط التحالف عدم رضا واحد لا عدم رضا كل منهما كما لا يخفى. وأشار بعجزهما إلى أن البيع ليس فيه خيار لأحدهما ولهذا قال في الخلاصة : إذا كان للمشتري خيار الرؤية أو خيار عيب أو خيار شرط لا يتحالفان .اهـ. والبائع كالمشتري فالمقصود أن من له الخيار متمكن من الفسخ فلا حاجة إلى التحالف ولكن ينبغي أن البائع إذا كان يدعي لزيادة الثمن وأنكرها المشتري فإن خيار المشتري التحالف، وأما خيار البائع .فلا ولو كان المشتري يدعي زيادة المبيع والبائع ينكرها فإن يمنع خيار البائع بمنعه لتمكنه من الفسخ وأما خيار المشتري فلا هذا ما ظهر لي تخريجاً لا نقلاً. وفي الخلاصة معزياً إلى الفتاوى : رجل اشترى عبداً ثم اختلف البائع والمشتري في الثمن فقال البائع إن كنت بعته إلا بألف درهم فهو حر، وقال المشتري إن كنت اشتريته إلا بخمسمائة درهم فهو حر، فالبيع لازم ولا يعتق العبد ويلزمه من الثمن ما أقر به المشتري لأنه منكر للزيادة لأن البائع أقر أن العبد قد عتق فلا يمكن نقضه بعد العتق ولا يعتق لأن المشتري منكر للعتق اهـ. وقيد بالاختلاف في القدر لأنهما لو اختلفا فقال البائع بعته بالميتة وقال المشتري اشتريته بالدراهم فالقول قول البائع لأنه إنكار للبيع كما لو قال طلقت وأنا ؛ كذا الخلاصة . في الاختلاف في القدر ما في الخلاصة معزياً إلى المحيط قال أبو سليمان: سمعت أبا يوسف فيمن باع طعاماً بعينه بعشرة وقال بعتك جزافاً بعشرة وقال المشتري اشتريت مكايلة يتحالفان وكذا كل ما يكال أو يوزن ولو كان هذا في ثوب فقال بعت ولم أسم ذرعاً وقال المشتري اشتريت مذارعة القول قول البائع، ولو قال اشتريت على أنه كذا وكذا ذراعاً كل ذراع بدرهم وقال البائع لم أسم ذراعاً فالقول قول المشتري ويتحالفان ويترادان على قول أبي يوسف ومحمد اهـ. وفي البزازية : اشترى مزبلة بخسمائة ثم ادعى أنه اشترى الأرض أيضاً والبائع يدعي أنه باع الكناسة فقط يحكم الثمن إن صلح لهما قضى بهما، وإن مثله لا يكون إلا ثمن الكناسة قضى بها فقط لا الأرض، وكذا الحكم في الرواية وعن محمد فيمن له أجمة تساوي ألفاً وفيها قصب يساوي ألفاً فباع الأجمة بعشرة مع
His statement: (And if they are unable and do not consent to the claim of one of them, they take mutual oaths) meaning the judge administers an oath to each of them regarding the other's claim. If it is before possession, it is analogical because each is a denier. As for after possession, it is only istiḥsān because the buyer does not claim anything since the sold item is in his possession; only the seller's claim of an increase in price remains and the buyer denies it, so his oath suffices. But we know it from the text: his statement, peace be upon him: 'If the two contracting parties differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return (the purchase).' He restricted by their lack of consent to indicate that the judge says to each: either you accept the claim of your companion, or else we will rescind it, because the purpose is to cut off the dispute, and this is a means to it because perhaps they would not be pleased with rescission, but when they know about it they will consent. And if he had said 'and neither of them consents to the claim of his companion' instead of his saying 'and they do not consent' it would be better, because the condition for mutual oaths is the lack of consent of one, not the lack of consent of both, as is not hidden. And he indicated by 'their inability' that the sale does not have an option for either of them; for this reason he said in al-Khulāṣah: if the buyer has an option of sight, or an option of defect, or an option of condition, they do not take mutual oaths. End. And the seller is like the buyer. So the point is that whoever has an option is able to rescind, so there is no need for mutual oaths. But it should be that if the seller claims an increase in price and the buyer denies it, then the buyer's option prevents mutual oaths? And as for the seller's option, no. And if the buyer claims an increase in the sold item and the seller denies it, then the seller's option prevents him because he is able to rescind, and as for the buyer's option, no. This is what appeared to me as an extraction, not as a narration. And in al-Khulāṣah, attributed to al-Fatāwā: a man bought a slave, then the seller and buyer differed in the price; the seller said, 'If I sold him except for a thousand dirhams, he is free,' and the buyer said, 'If I bought him except for five hundred dirhams, he is free.' The sale is binding and the slave does not become free, and he is obliged for the price that the buyer acknowledged, because he is a denier of the increase, because the seller acknowledged that the slave has become free, so it cannot be nullified after emancipation, and he does not become free because the buyer is a denier of emancipation. End. And it is restricted to difference in the amount, because if they differ and the seller says, 'I sold him for two hundred' and the buyer says, 'I bought him for dirhams,' then the statement is that of the seller, because it is a denial of the sale, as if he said, 'I divorced and I...' Thus in al-Khulāṣah. Regarding the difference in amount, what is in al-Khulāṣah attributed to al-Muḥīṭ: Abu Sulayman said: I heard Abu Yusuf about someone who sold specific food for ten and said, 'I sold you lump sum for ten,' and the buyer said, 'I bought by measure,' they take mutual oaths. Similarly, everything that is measured or weighed. If this is in a garment, and he said, 'I sold without mentioning the yardage,' and the buyer said, 'I bought by the yard,' the statement is that of the seller. If he said, 'I bought on the condition that it is such and such yards, each yard for a dirham,' and the seller said, 'I did not mention the yardage,' then the statement is that of the buyer, and they take mutual oaths and return (the purchase) according to the saying of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. End. And in al-Bazzāziyyah: He bought a dunghill for five hundred, then he claimed that he also bought the land, and the seller claims that he only sold the garbage; if the price is suitable for both, it is judged for both; if its like is only the price of the garbage, then it is judged only for the garbage, not the land. Similarly, the ruling in the narration. And from Muhammad: regarding someone who has a thicket worth a thousand and in it reeds worth a thousand, so he sold the thicket for ten with...
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is the commentary on the matn's ruling about mutual oaths (tahaluf) when both buyer and seller are unable to provide evidence and do not accept each other's claim. The author explains that the judge administers an oath to each party regarding the other's claim. Before possession, this is based on qiyas (analogy) because each is a denier; after possession, it is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because the buyer has possession and does not claim anything. The proof is the hadith: 'If the two contracting parties differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return the purchase.' The condition is that neither party has an option (like option of sight, defect, or condition) because the one with an option can simply rescind the contract, making oaths unnecessary. The author then discusses a case where the buyer has an option versus the seller having an option, giving his own understanding. Next, he presents a case from al-Khulasah where the parties make conditional statements about emancipation; the ruling is that the slave does not become free and the price is what the buyer acknowledges. The passage then covers differences in the type of price (lump sum vs. measure) and gives rulings on when mutual oaths are required. Finally, it treats a case from al-Bazzaziyya about buying a dunghill and land, and one from Muhammad about selling a thicket with reeds. The text cuts off at the end.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oaths taken by two parties in a sale dispute when both lack evidence and do not accept the other's claim, leading to rescission of the contract.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that deviates from strict analogy in favor of a more equitable outcome.
خيار (khiyār): An option or right to rescind a contract, such as option of sight, option of defect, or option of condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for mutual oaths (tahaluf) according to the passage?
A) The commodity must be perishable.
B) Neither party has an option (khiyar).
C) The price must be in cash.
D) Both parties must be present in court.

Key Takeaway
Mutual oaths are wajib when both parties lack evidence and have no option, based on the hadith and istihsan.

Conditions and Exceptions to Mutual Oaths
Expansion on conditions and exceptions for tahaluf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that prevent mutual oaths (tahaluf) and apply the rulings to various sale scenarios.

Core Idea
Mutual oaths are not needed if either party has an option, and disputes over price type vs. amount require different applications.

This lesson explores the conditions that must be present for tahaluf to apply, as well as the exceptions. The text discusses how options (khiyar) prevent tahaluf, and then moves to specific cases: conditional emancipation statements, differences in the nature of the price (lump sum vs. measure), and disputes over the subject matter (e.g., dunghill and land). Understanding these details is crucial for correctly applying the rule.

Guided Path
1
List the conditions for tahaluf
From the text: inability to produce evidence, lack of consent to the other's claim, commodity still in existence, and no khiyar for either party.
If any condition is missing, tahaluf is not performed.
2
Identify when tahaluf is not required
If either buyer or seller has an option (sight, defect, condition), they can rescind directly, so no need for oaths.
The text also considers which party's option prevents tahaluf in specific claims.
3
Analyze the emancipation case
When each party makes a conditional emancipation statement, the slave does not become free, and the price is set by the buyer's acknowledgment.
This shows the interplay between tahaluf and conditional statements.
4
Distinguish between amount vs. type of price
If dispute is about the amount (e.g., lump sum vs. measured), tahaluf applies. If about the type of currency, the seller's word is taken.
This is based on the nature of denial.
5
Apply to various scenarios
Go through the examples: food (measured), garment (yardage), dunghill (price determination), thicket (mixed property).
See how the rulings vary based on the type of sale.

His statement: (And if they are unable and do not consent to the claim of one of them, they take mutual oaths) meaning the judge administers an oath to each of them regarding the other's claim. If it is before possession, it is analogical because each is a denier. As for after possession, it is only istiḥsān because the buyer does not claim anything since the sold item is in his possession; only the seller's claim of an increase in price remains and the buyer denies it, so his oath suffices. But we know it from the text: his statement, peace be upon him: 'If the two contracting parties differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return (the purchase).' He restricted by their lack of consent to indicate that the judge says to each: either you accept the claim of your companion, or else we will rescind it, because the purpose is to cut off the dispute, and this is a means to it because perhaps they would not be pleased with rescission, but when they know about it they will consent. And if he had said 'and neither of them consents to the claim of his companion' instead of his saying 'and they do not consent' it would be better, because the condition for mutual oaths is the lack of consent of one, not the lack of consent of both, as is not hidden. And he indicated by 'their inability' that the sale does not have an option for either of them; for this reason he said in al-Khulāṣah: if the buyer has an option of sight, or an option of defect, or an option of condition, they do not take mutual oaths. End. And the seller is like the buyer. So the point is that whoever has an option is able to rescind, so there is no need for mutual oaths. This passage is the commentary on the matn's ruling about mutual oaths (tahaluf) when both buyer and seller are unable to provide evidence and do not accept each other's claim. Key Terms
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oaths in a sale dispute.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru'ya): Option of sight – the buyer can rescind if he hasn't seen the item.
خيار العيب (khiyār al-'ayb): Option of defect – the buyer can return a defective item.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): Option of condition – the buyer/seller can stipulate a period to rescind.
