Tahaluf in Sale Disputes: Conditions, Procedure, and Proof
Explains when mutual oaths apply, starting with buyer's oath, and judge's rescission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for tahaluf, explain the order of oaths based on the Hanafi mufta bihi, and apply the ruling to disputes over price and sold item.

Core Idea
When buyer and seller disagree and neither can prove, the judge administers mutual oaths and rescinds the sale.

The following passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling of mutual oaths (tahaluf) when buyer and seller dispute over the price or the precise item sold, and the commodity still exists. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) provides the foundation, and the Hanafi school refines the procedure through qiyas and istihsan. You will learn the conditions that trigger tahaluf, the correct order of oaths, and how differences among Hanafi imams were resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its textual proof
Read the opening lines to understand when tahaluf becomes wajib. Note the hadith cited and how the Hanafi principle that al-amr yufidu al-wujub derives obligation.
The imperative تحالفا indicates obligation.
2
Analyze the conditions for tahaluf
The passage lists three conditions: inability to prove, refusal to accept the other's claim, and absence of any khiyar (option) for either party. Trace how these are derived from the hadith and from the nature of dispute.
If one has an option, he can rescind without oaths.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on oath order
Muhammad and later Abu Yusuf hold that the buyer's oath comes first; Abu Yusuf initially said the seller's oath. Understand the reasoning on both sides: buyer's stronger denial vs. the hadith ظاهر‌ه.
The mufta bihi is buyer's oath first for price disputes.
4
Understand the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the buyer is sworn first and refuses (nukul), the price is imposed immediately. If the seller were first, delivery of the sold item would be delayed. See how the order directly affects the outcome.
This practical effect justifies the preferred view.
5
Apply to nuanced cases
Work through the examples from al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, and al-Bazzaziyya: disputes over measured items, options, third-party claims. See where tahaluf applies or when the price is decisive (tahkim al-thaman).
Tahkim al-thaman is outside zahir al-riwaya and not relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف
عجزا ولم يرضيا بدعوى أحدهما تحالفا وبدأ بيمين المشتري وفسخ القاضي بطلب
.
قوله : (وإن عجزا ولم يرضيا بدعوى أحدهما تحالفا أي استحلف الحاكم كل واحد منهما على دعوى صاحبه، فإن كان قبل القبض فهو قياسي لأن كلاً منهما منكر، وأما بعده فاستحساني فقط لأن المشتري لا يدعي شيئاً لأن المبيع سالم له بقي دعوى البائع في زيادة الثمن والمشتري ينكره فيكتفي بحلفه لكنا عرفناه بالنص وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إذا اختلف المتبايعان والسلعة قائمة بعينها تحالفا وترادا قيد بعدم رضاهما للإشارة إلى أن القاضي يقول لكل منهما إما أن ترضى بدعوى صاحبك وإلا فسخناه لأن المقصود قطع المنازعة وهذا جهة فيه لأنه ربما لا يرضيان بالفسخ فإذا علما به يتراضيان. ولو قال ولم يرض واحد منهما بدعوى صاحبه بدل قوله ولم يرضيا لكان أولى لأن شرط التحالف عدم رضا واحد لا عدم رضا كل منهما كما لا يخفى. وأشار بعجزهما إلى أن البيع ليس فيه خيار لأحدهما ولهذا قال في الخلاصة : إذا كان للمشتري خيار الرؤية أو خيار عيب أو خيار شرط لا يتحالفان .اهـ. والبائع كالمشتري فالمقصود أن من له الخيار متمكن من الفسخ فلا حاجة إلى التحالف ولكن ينبغي أن البائع إذا كان يدعي لزيادة الثمن وأنكرها المشتري فإن خيار المشتري التحالف، وأما خيار البائع .فلا ولو كان المشتري يدعي زيادة المبيع والبائع ينكرها فإن يمنع خيار البائع بمنعه لتمكنه من الفسخ وأما خيار المشتري فلا هذا ما ظهر لي تخريجاً لا نقلاً. وفي الخلاصة معزياً إلى الفتاوى : رجل اشترى عبداً ثم اختلف البائع والمشتري في الثمن فقال البائع إن كنت بعته إلا بألف درهم فهو حر، وقال المشتري إن كنت اشتريته إلا بخمسمائة درهم فهو حر، فالبيع لازم ولا يعتق العبد ويلزمه من الثمن ما أقر به المشتري لأنه منكر للزيادة لأن البائع أقر أن العبد قد عتق فلا يمكن نقضه بعد العتق ولا يعتق لأن المشتري منكر للعتق اهـ. وقيد بالاختلاف في القدر لأنهما لو اختلفا فقال البائع بعته بالميتة وقال المشتري اشتريته بالدراهم فالقول قول البائع لأنه إنكار للبيع كما لو قال طلقت وأنا ؛ كذا الخلاصة . في الاختلاف في القدر ما في الخلاصة معزياً إلى المحيط قال أبو سليمان: سمعت أبا يوسف فيمن باع طعاماً بعينه بعشرة وقال بعتك جزافاً بعشرة وقال المشتري اشتريت مكايلة يتحالفان وكذا كل ما يكال أو يوزن ولو كان هذا في ثوب فقال بعت ولم أسم ذرعاً وقال المشتري اشتريت مذارعة القول قول البائع، ولو قال اشتريت على أنه كذا وكذا ذراعاً كل ذراع بدرهم وقال البائع لم أسم ذراعاً فالقول قول المشتري ويتحالفان ويترادان على قول أبي يوسف ومحمد اهـ. وفي البزازية : اشترى مزبلة بخمسمائة ثم ادعى أنه اشترى الأرض أيضاً والبائع يدعي أنه باع الكناسة فقط يحكم الثمن إن صلح لهما قضى بهما، وإن مثله لا يكون إلا ثمن الكناسة قضى بها فقط لا الأرض، وكذا الحكم في الرواية وعن محمد فيمن له أجمة تساوي ألفاً وفيها قصب يساوي ألفاً فباع الأجمة بعشرة مع صبي؛ الماء ومن

٣٧٤
كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف
آلاف ثم ادعى المشتري وقوع العقد على الأصل والبائع وقوع العقد على القصب أن العقد يفسد. ولو اشترى سرجاً وادعى أنه اشتراه بركابه أو خاتماً وادعى أنه بفصه وأنكر البائع يتحالفان ويترادان والبقالي اختلفا في الثياب والجراب والنخلة والرطب وادعى البائع أحدهما والمشتري كليهما يحكم الثمن، فإذا استويا في العادة لم يجز، وعن الإمام فيمن اشترى عبداً بألف وقبضه وقبض البائع الثمن ثم زعم المشتري أنه كان مع العبد أمة بعينها دخلت في البيع وأنكره البائع يحلف بالله ما باعه الأمة معه ولا يرد شيئاً من الثمن. وقال الثاني بعد الحلف يرد عليه حصة الأمة من الثمن في الاستحسان، وكذا في كل ما يكون مثله في البيع فإذا كان شيئاً لا يكون مثله في البيع لا يصدق .اهـ. وبهذا ظهر أن التحالف عند اختلافهما في قدر المبيع عند عدم تحكيم الثمن، أما إذا حكم الثمن فلا تحالف والظاهر أن تحكيم الثمن خارج عن ظاهر الرواية فلا يعتمد عليه في المذهب. ثم اعلم أن التحالف في البيع لم ينحصر في الاختلاف في الثمن أو المبيع بل يجري في كل موضع يكون كل منهما مدعياً أو منكراً لما ذكره في الكافي : باع أمة وتقابضا فقال البائع هي لزيد أمرني ببيعها، وقال زيد بعتها منك بمائة دينار وقبضتها وبعت ملكك فهي للمشتري وتحالفا لأن البائع يدعي الأمر بالبيع والمقر له ينكر والمقر له يدعي عليه الثمن وهو ينكر ؛ وإن حلفا فإن جهلت أنها للمقر له وكذبهما المشتري ضمن المقر قيمتها للمقر ،له وإن كانت معروفة أنها للمقر له لا ضمان اهـ .

وهو
قوله: (وبدأ بيمين المشتري) وهذا قول محمد وأبي يوسف آخراً وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح، لأن المشتري أشدهما إنكاراً لأنه يطالب أولاً بالثمن أو لأنه يتعجل فائدة النكول وهو إلزام الثمن ولو بدأ بيمين البائع تتأخر المطالبة بتسليم المبيع إلى زمان استيفاء الثمن، وكان أبو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى يقول أولاً يبدأ بيمين البائع لقوله عليه السلام «إذا اختلف المتبايعان فالقول ما قاله البائع خصه بالذكر وأقل فائدته التقديم. أطلقه ببيع العين بالدين، أما في بيع العين بالعين أو الدين بالدين فالقاضي مخير للاستواء . وصفة اليمين أن يحلف البائع بالله تعالى ما باعه بألف ويحلف المشتري بالله ما اشتراه بألفين . وقال في الزيادات: يحلف بالله ما باعه بألف ولقد باعه بألفين ويحلف المشتري بالله ما اشتراه بألفين ولقد اشتراه بألف يضم الإثبات إلى النفي تأكيداً، والأصح الاقتصار على النفي لأن الأيمان على ذلك وضعت دل عليه حديث القسامة بالله ما قتلتم ولا علمتم له قاتلا». وفي شرح التلخيص من باب الاختلاف فيما يجب للبائع على المشتري وبالعكس مسألة الأصح فيها تقديم يمين البائع قوله : ( وفسخ القاضي بطلب أحدهما فلا ينفسخ البيع بحلفهما) لأنه لم قول المصنف : ( وبدأ بيمين المشتري قال الرملي : هذا إذا كان الاختلاف في الثمن، أما لو كان في المبيع يبدأ بيمين البائع كما يستفاد مما يأتي في الاختلاف في الإجارة تأمل اهـ . قلت : ووجهه
Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oaths
... they are unable [to prove] and do not accept the claim of one of them, they take mutual oaths, and the judge begins with the oath of the buyer, and the judge rescinds [the sale] upon request.

His saying: (And if they are unable and do not accept the claim of one of them, they take mutual oaths) i.e., the judge administers an oath to each of them regarding the claim of the other. If [the dispute] is before possession, it is by analogy (qiyasi) because each of them is a denier. After possession, it is only by juristic preference (istihsani) because the buyer does not claim anything since the sold item is safely in his possession; only the seller's claim of an increase in price remains, and the buyer denies it, so his oath suffices. But we know it by the text: his saying (peace be upon him): 'If the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return [the goods].' He conditioned [tahaluf] on their lack of consent to indicate that the judge says to each: 'Either you accept the claim of your companion, or else we rescind [the sale],' because the purpose is to end the dispute. There is a consideration here because perhaps they would not agree to rescission, but when they know [the judge will do it], they may agree. If he had said 'and not one of them accepts the claim of his companion' instead of 'and they do not accept', it would be better because the condition for tahaluf is the lack of acceptance by one, not lack of acceptance by both, as is clear. And his indication of their inability points to the fact that the sale does not have an option (khiyar) for either of them. Hence it is said in al-Khulasa: 'If the buyer has the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya), option of defect (khiyar al-ayb), or option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart), they do not take mutual oaths.' Similarly for the seller. The point is that whoever has an option is able to rescind, so there is no need for tahaluf. However, it should be noted that if the seller claims an increase in the price and the buyer denies it, then the buyer's option [prevents?] tahaluf. As for the seller's option, it does not. And if the buyer claims an increase in the sold item and the seller denies it, then the seller's option prevents [tahaluf] because he can rescind. As for the buyer's option, it does not. This is what appears to me as derivation (takhrij), not transmission. In al-Khulasa, attributed to al-Fatawa: A man bought a slave, then the seller and buyer differed over the price. The seller said: 'If I sold it except for a thousand dirhams, then it is free.' The buyer said: 'If I bought it except for five hundred dirhams, then it is free.' The sale is binding, and the slave is not freed. The buyer is liable for the price he admitted because he denies the increase, and the seller admitted that the slave is freed, so it cannot be revoked after emancipation, but the slave is not freed because the buyer denies the emancipation.
And he restricted it to dispute over the amount because if they differed such that the seller said 'I sold it with a dead [animal]' and the buyer said 'I bought it with dirhams', then the seller's word is taken because he denies the sale, just as if he said 'I divorced while I was...' al-Khulasa. Regarding dispute over the amount, in al-Khulasa attributed to al-Muhit: Abu Sulayman said: I heard Abu Yusuf regarding someone who sold a specific food for ten and said 'I sold it by guess (juzafan) for ten', and the buyer said 'I bought it by measure (mukayilan)'; they take mutual oaths. Similarly for everything measured or weighed. If it were about a garment: the seller said 'I sold it without mentioning the length', and the buyer said 'I bought it by length (mudhara'atan)'; the seller's word is taken. If the buyer said 'I bought it on the basis that it is such-and-such cubits, each cubit for a dirham', and the seller said 'I did not mention the cubits', then the buyer's word is taken, and they take mutual oaths and return [the goods] according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
In al-Bazzaziyya: [If] someone bought a rubbish dump for five hundred, then claimed he also bought the land, and the seller claims he only sold the dung: the price is decisive (yahkum al-thaman). If it fits both, it is judged for both; if such an amount can only be the price of the dung, it is judged for it only, not the land. Similarly in the narration. And from Muhammad regarding someone who had a reed-bed worth a thousand and in it reeds worth a thousand, and he sold the reed-bed for ten thousand, then the buyer claimed the contract was on the land and the seller claimed it was on the reeds: the contract is void. If someone bought a saddle and claimed he bought it with its stirrups, or a ring and claimed it with its gem, and the seller denies, they take mutual oaths and return the goods. Al-Baqqali: they differed over garments and the bag (jirab), and the palm tree and the ripe dates: the seller claimed one and the buyer claimed both; the price is decisive. If they are equal in custom, it is not permissible. From al-Imam regarding someone who bought a slave for a thousand, took possession, and the seller took the price, then the buyer claimed that there was a slave-girl with the slave included in the sale, and the seller denies: the seller swears by Allah that he did not sell the slave-girl with him, and does not return any part of the price. Al-Thani said: after swearing, he returns the share of the slave-girl from the price, based on istihsan. Similarly for everything that is customarily sold together. If it is something not customarily sold together, he is not believed. From this it appears that tahaluf occurs when they differ over the amount of the sold item in the absence of price being decisive (tahkim al-thaman). If the price is decisive, there is no tahaluf. It seems that tahkim al-thaman is outside the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), so it is not relied upon in the school. Then know that tahaluf in sale is not restricted to dispute over price or sold item; rather it applies in every situation where each of them is a claimant or denier, as mentioned in al-Kafi: He sold a slave-girl and they exchanged possession; the seller said: 'She is Zayd's; he ordered me to sell her', and Zayd said: 'I sold her to you for one hundred dinars and I took possession and I sold your property'; the slave-girl is for the buyer, and they take mutual oaths because the seller claims he was ordered to sell, and the recognized owner (al-muqarr lahu) denies, and the recognized owner claims the price from him and he denies. If they both swear and it is unknown that she belongs to the recognized owner and the buyer denies him, then the one who recognized (al-muqir) is liable for her value to the recognized owner. If it is known that she belongs to the recognized owner, there is no liability.

His saying: (And he begins with the oath of the buyer) This is the view of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf later, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa, and it is the correct one, because the buyer is the one most in denial (ashaduhuma inkaaran) since he is first demanded for the price, or because he quickly gets the benefit of the nukul (refusal to swear) which is the imposition of the price. If he began with the seller's oath, the demand for delivering the sold item would be delayed until the time of receiving the price. Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) initially said to begin with the seller's oath due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'When the two parties to a sale differ, the word is that of the seller.' He specified it by mention, and the least benefit is giving precedence. He gave it unrestricted in the sale of an 'ayn for a debt. As for sale of an 'ayn for an 'ayn or debt for debt, the judge has a choice due to equality. The description of the oath: the seller swears by Allah most High 'I did not sell it for a thousand' and the buyer swears 'I did not buy it for two thousand'. In al-Ziyadat: He swears 'By Allah, I did not sell it for a thousand, and indeed I sold it for two thousand', and the buyer swears 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand, and indeed I bought it for a thousand'—adding affirmation to negation for emphasis. The most correct is to restrict to negation because oaths are established for that; the hadith of Qasama indicates it: 'By Allah, you did not kill him, nor do you know his killer.' In Sharh al-Talkhis, from the chapter of dispute over what is due to the seller from the buyer and vice versa, the issue: the most correct opinion is to give precedence to the seller's oath.
His saying: (And the judge rescinds at the request of one of them, so the sale is not rescinded by their swearing) because... The author's saying: (And he begins with the oath of the buyer) al-Ramli said: This is if the dispute is over the price. If it is over the sold item, he begins with the seller's oath, as understood from what follows in the dispute over hire. Consider. I say: its reasoning is...
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the chapter of mutual oaths (tahaluf) in sale disputes. The author explains the conditions and rulings for when buyer and seller disagree about the price or the exact item sold, and the commodity still exists. The core ruling is that if both parties are unable to prove their claim and neither accepts the other's claim, the judge administers oaths to both and then rescinds the sale. This is based on the hadith: 'If the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return [the goods].'

The commentary then delves into nuances: whether the dispute is before or after possession, the effect of options (khiyar), and the procedural order of oaths. The author supports the view that the buyer's oath should be administered first, as per Muhammad and Abu Yusuf's later opinion, because the buyer is the more vigorous denier (he is being asked to pay the price). There is also a discussion of the precise wording of the oath—whether to add an affirmative statement or only a negative. The author considers the more correct view to be restricting to negation based on the hadith of Qasama.

Several case studies are presented from earlier Hanafi works (al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, al-Bazzaziyya) to illustrate when tahaluf applies or not, and when the price is decisive (tahkim al-thaman). Notably, the author warns that tahkim al-thaman is not part of the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) and thus not relied upon in the madhhab. The passage also covers scenarios where a third party is involved, such as a slave sold by order of the owner, and the complexities of oaths and liability.

Overall, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of integrating textual proof (hadith), qiyas, istihsan, and detailed case analysis to arrive at a balanced procedure for resolving sale disputes that avoids false oaths and achieves justice.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oaths administered by the judge to both parties when they disagree and neither can prove their claim, leading to rescission of the sale.
يمين (yamīn): An oath sworn by Allah, used to affirm or deny a claim in the absence of evidence.
قياسي (qiyāsī): Ruling derived by analogy from a primary text or principle.
استحساني (istiḥsānī): Ruling based on juristic preference, deviating from qiyas due to a stronger consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, whose oath is administered first when the dispute is about the price?
A) The seller's oath is first
B) The buyer's oath is first
C) The judge chooses arbitrarily
D) Both oaths are administered simultaneously

Key Takeaway
Tahaluf is a wajib procedure when parties dispute and cannot prove; the buyer's oath is first for price disputes, but the seller's oath is first for disputes over the sold item.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Dispute over the Sold Item: Mutual Oaths and Price Settlement
Dispute resolution rules in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of disputes over the sold item and state whether the ruling is contract invalidation, mutual oaths, or price settlement.

Core Idea
When the sold item is disputed, the ruling depends on whether the price can settle the ambiguity; if not, mutual oaths and rescission.

In this lesson, we examine several cases from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq where buyer and seller disagree on the exact subject matter. The author presents a spectrum: from complete invalidity when the item is wholly uncertain, to mutual oaths when an accessory is disputed, to price-based settlement when the price clarifies the claim. We will trace the underlying principle and its limitations according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Classify the dispute
Identify whether the dispute is over the very substance of the sold item (e.g., sugar cane vs. reed) or over an accessory (e.g., saddle vs. saddle with pommel).
If the dispute is about the core item, the contract is invalid (fasid).
2
Check if the price clarifies
When the dispute involves an accessory or a set of items, see if the price paid matches one party's claim. If the price typically corresponds to one version, the price 'judges' (tahkim al-thaman).
This rule is applied in the baqqali (coarse cloth) cases.
3
Apply price settlement or mutual oaths
If the price clearly supports one claim, that party is believed. Otherwise, both parties take oaths (tahāluf) and the contract is rescinded.
Price settlement is not from zahir al-riwaya; many later Hanafi scholars do not rely on it.
4
Distinguish the accessory rule
When the dispute is about an accessory that is typically sold separately (like a female slave with a slave), the buyer is not automatically believed; the seller swears and keeps the full price (Abu Hanifa's view).
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require refund by istihsan for such accessories.

Source Text

قالف ثم ادعى المشتري وقوع العقد على الأصل والبائع وقوع العقد على القصب أن العقد يفسد. ولو اشترى سرجاً وادعى أنه اشتراه بركابه أو خاتماً وادعى أنه بفصه وأنكر البائع يتحالفان ويترادان والبقالي اختلفا في الثياب والجراب والنخلة والرطب وادعى البائع أحدهما والمشتري كليهما يحكم الثمن، فإذا استويا في العادة لم يجز، وعن الإمام فيمن اشترى عبداً بألف وقبضه وقبض البائع الثمن ثم زعم المشتري أنه كان مع العبد أمة بعينها دخلت في البيع وأنكره البائع يحلف بالله ما باعه الأمة معه ولا يرد شيئاً من الثمن. وقال الثاني بعد الحلف يرد عليه حصة الأمة من الثمن في الاستحسان، وكذا في كل ما يكون مثله في البيع فإذا كان شيئاً لا يكون مثله في البيع لا يصدق .اهـ. وبهذا ظهر أن التحالف عند اختلافهما في قدر المبيع عند عدم تحكيم الثمن، أما إذا حكم الثمن فلا تحالف والظاهر أن تحكيم الثمن خارج عن ظاهر الرواية فلا يعتمد عليه في المذهب.
He said: Then if the buyer claims the contract occurred on the original (sugar cane) and the seller claims it occurred on the reed, the contract becomes invalid. And if one buys a saddle and claims he bought it with its pommel, or a ring and claims it with its setting, and the seller denies, they take mutual oaths and return the goods. And for coarse cloth (baqqali), if they differ over the clothes and the bag and the palm tree and the fresh dates, the seller claims one and the buyer claims both, the price decides. If they are equal in custom, it is not permissible. And from Imam Abu Hanifa: regarding one who buys a slave for one thousand and takes possession, and the seller takes the price, then the buyer claims that along with the slave there was a specific female slave included in the sale and the seller denies, then the seller swears by Allah that he did not sell the female slave with him and does not return anything of the price. And the second (Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) said: after the oath, he returns the corresponding portion of the price for the female slave, by istihsan. Similarly, for everything that is like that in sale, but if it is something that is not like that in sale, he is not believed. (end quote). By this it becomes apparent that mutual oaths apply when they differ over the quantity of the sold item while the price does not settle it. But if the price settles it, there is no mutual oath. And it appears that the settlement by price is outside the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), so it is not relied upon in the school.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents several cases of dispute over the exact subject matter of a sale. The author (from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) expands on a matn by al-Kanz. The first case: buyer says the contract was on sugar cane (asl), seller says on reed (qasab). Since the exact item is disputed, the contract is invalid because it is impossible to determine which was intended without further evidence. Then cases of a saddle (with or without pommel) and a ring (with or without setting): the buyer and seller disagree on the inclusion of an accessory. Here, they take mutual oaths and return the goods because the accessory is a distinct entity. Then the case of coarse cloth (baqqali) and other items: the seller claims one item (e.g., just the clothes), the buyer claims both (clothes and bag). In these cases, the price determines the correct claim—if the price matches the seller's claim, his word is taken; if it matches the buyer's, his word is taken. If the price is ambiguous, mutual oaths apply. Then a specific ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa: a buyer buys a slave, takes possession, then claims the sale included a specific female slave (umat bi-aynihi). The seller denies. Abu Hanifa says the seller swears he did not sell her and does not refund anything. But the 'second' (probably Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) says by istihsan he must refund the portion of the price corresponding to the female slave. The passage ends with a general rule: this refund applies only to items that are typically sold separately; if not, the buyer is not believed. The commentator adds that from these examples, it is clear that mutual oaths occur only when the price does not decide the dispute. However, the ruling that the price decides is not from the authoritative zahir al-riwaya, so it is not the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
تحكيم الثمن (tahkīm al-thaman): Using the price as a judge to determine which party's claim is correct when the commodity is disputed.
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oaths taken by buyer and seller when they disagree on the terms of the contract, leading to rescission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a buyer claims he bought a slave with a specific female slave included, and the seller denies, what is the mufta bihi according to the text?
A) The seller swears and keeps the full price.
B) The seller swears and refunds the portion for the female slave.
C) The buyer and seller take mutual oaths.
D) The buyer is believed automatically.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over the sold item, the Hanafi school primarily relies on mutual oaths, while price settlement (tahkim al-thaman) is considered weak and not part of the core transmission.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Refund for Unspecified Accessory
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on refund for accessory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding refund for an unspecified accessory and identify the illa for the exception.

Core Idea
When an accessory is claimed but not specified in the contract, Abu Hanifa rejects refund; the Sahibayn grant refund by istihsan for items typically sold separately.

This lesson focuses on a specific case within the passage: a buyer purchases a slave and later claims a female slave was included. The seller denies. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on whether the seller must refund part of the price. We will analyze their proofs and the underlying qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa says the seller swears and does not refund anything because the female slave was not mentioned in the contract; the buyer's claim is an addition that cannot be enforced.
This is based on the principle that the contract is limited to what was explicitly agreed.
2
Identify the ruling of Abu Yusuf/Muhammad
They say the seller must refund the portion of the price corresponding to the female slave by istihsan, because it is common to include a female slave with a slave.
Istihsan here overrides the strict qiyas.
3
Analyze the illa for the exception
The text says: 'Similarly, for everything that is like that in sale'—i.e., items typically sold together. The illa is customary inclusion (urf).
If the item is not typically sold together (e.g., a carpet with a horse), the buyer is not believed.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
According to the commentator, Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position in the madhhab.
This is because the Sahibayn's view is based on istihsan, and the stricter rule is preferred when the accessory was not specified.

Source Text

قالف ثم ادعى المشتري وقوع العقد على الأصل والبائع وقوع العقد على القصب أن العقد يفسد. ولو اشترى سرجاً وادعى أنه اشتراه بركابه أو خاتماً وادعى أنه بفصه وأنكر البائع يتحالفان ويترادان والبقالي اختلفا في الثياب والجراب والنخلة والرطب وادعى البائع أحدهما والمشتري كليهما يحكم الثمن، فإذا استويا في العادة لم يجز، وعن الإمام فيمن اشترى عبداً بألف وقبضه وقبض البائع الثمن ثم زعم المشتري أنه كان مع العبد أمة بعينها دخلت في البيع وأنكره البائع يحلف بالله ما باعه الأمة معه ولا يرد شيئاً من الثمن. وقال الثاني بعد الحلف يرد عليه حصة الأمة من الثمن في الاستحسان، وكذا في كل ما يكون مثله في البيع فإذا كان شيئاً لا يكون مثله في البيع لا يصدق .اهـ. وبهذا ظهر أن التحالف عند اختلافهما في قدر المبيع عند عدم تحكيم الثمن، أما إذا حكم الثمن فلا تحالف والظاهر أن تحكيم الثمن خارج عن ظاهر الرواية فلا يعتمد عليه في المذهب.
He said: Then if the buyer claims the contract occurred on the original (sugar cane) and the seller claims it occurred on the reed, the contract becomes invalid. And if one buys a saddle and claims he bought it with its pommel, or a ring and claims it with its setting, and the seller denies, they take mutual oaths and return the goods. And for coarse cloth (baqqali), if they differ over the clothes and the bag and the palm tree and the fresh dates, the seller claims one and the buyer claims both, the price decides. If they are equal in custom, it is not permissible. And from Imam Abu Hanifa: regarding one who buys a slave for one thousand and takes possession, and the seller takes the price, then the buyer claims that along with the slave there was a specific female slave included in the sale and the seller denies, then the seller swears by Allah that he did not sell the female slave with him and does not return anything of the price. And the second (Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) said: after the oath, he returns the corresponding portion of the price for the female slave, by istihsan. Similarly, for everything that is like that in sale, but if it is something that is not like that in sale, he is not believed. (end quote). By this it becomes apparent that mutual oaths apply when they differ over the quantity of the sold item while the price does not settle it. But if the price settles it, there is no mutual oath. And it appears that the settlement by price is outside the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), so it is not relied upon in the school.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents several cases of dispute over the exact subject matter of a sale. The author (from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) expands on a matn by al-Kanz. The first case: buyer says the contract was on sugar cane (asl), seller says on reed (qasab). Since the exact item is disputed, the contract is invalid because it is impossible to determine which was intended without further evidence. Then cases of a saddle (with or without pommel) and a ring (with or without setting): the buyer and seller disagree on the inclusion of an accessory. Here, they take mutual oaths and return the goods because the accessory is a distinct entity. Then the case of coarse cloth (baqqali) and other items: the seller claims one item (e.g., just the clothes), the buyer claims both (clothes and bag). In these cases, the price determines the correct claim—if the price matches the seller's claim, his word is taken; if it matches the buyer's, his word is taken. If the price is ambiguous, mutual oaths apply. Then a specific ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa: a buyer buys a slave, takes possession, then claims the sale included a specific female slave (umat bi-aynihi). The seller denies. Abu Hanifa says the seller swears he did not sell her and does not refund anything. But the 'second' (probably Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) says by istihsan he must refund the portion of the price corresponding to the female slave. The passage ends with a general rule: this refund applies only to items that are typically sold separately; if not, the buyer is not believed. The commentator adds that from these examples, it is clear that mutual oaths occur only when the price does not decide the dispute. However, the ruling that the price decides is not from the authoritative zahir al-riwaya, so it is not the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference that departs from strict analogy in favor of a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for the Sahibayn's istihsan to apply in the accessory refund case?
A) The buyer must swear an oath.
B) The accessory must be typically sold together with the main item.
C) The price must be settled by custom.
D) The seller must admit the accessory.

Key Takeaway
In intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's strict position: no refund for unspecified accessories, even if custom supports inclusion.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Mutual Oaths Beyond Price and Commodity
Establishes general scope of mutual oaths in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that mutual oaths (tahāluf) apply whenever each party is both claimant and denier, and apply it to a sales dispute involving agency.

Core Idea
Tahāluf applies wherever both parties are simultaneously claimants and deniers — not just over price or commodity.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author expands the scope of mutual oaths in sales. While many assume tahāluf only occurs when there is disagreement over the price or the item, the Hanafi school holds that the underlying rationale is the duality of claim and denial. Let's examine the text and the illustrative example from al-Kafi to understand how this principle works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Read the first sentence: 'التحالف في البيع لم ينحصر في الاختلاف في الثمن أو المبيع بل يجري في كل موضع يكون كل منهما مدعياً أو منكراً'. State the principle in your own words.
The key phrase is 'كل موضع يكون كل منهما مدعياً أو منكراً'.
2
Decompose the Example
Consider the scenario from al-Kafi: a seller sells a slave, then claims he sold on behalf of Zaid; Zaid claims he sold directly. Map each party's claims and denials. Who is muddaʿi and who is munkir for each issue?
The seller claims authorization (muddaʿi) and denies Zaid's ownership (munkir). Zaid claims the price (muddaʿi) and denies the seller's authority (munkir).
3
Extract the Illah (Effective Cause)
Why does tahāluf apply? Because each party is both a claimant and a denier on different aspects of the dispute. This dual role triggers the procedure, not the specific subject of the dispute.
The illah is the presence of mutual muddaʿī wa munkir status, which is present in more than just price/commodity disputes.
4
Apply the Principle to a New Scenario
Consider: Buyer and seller disagree about whether the contract was for cash or credit. Each has a claim and denial. Determine whether tahāluf applies using the principle learned.
Yes, because both are muddaʿī and munkir simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن التحالف في البيع لم ينحصر في الاختلاف في الثمن أو المبيع بل يجري في كل موضع يكون كل منهما مدعياً أو منكراً لما ذكره في الكافي : باع أمة وتقابضا فقال البائع هي لزيد أمرني ببيعها، وقال زيد بعتها منك بمائة دينار وقبضتها وبعت ملكك فهي للمشتري وتحالفا لأن البائع يدعي الأمر بالبيع والمقر له ينكر والمقر له يدعي عليه الثمن وهو ينكر ؛ وإن حلفا فإن جهلت أنها للمقر له وكذبهما المشتري ضمن المقر قيمتها للمقر له وإن كانت معروفة أنها للمقر له لا ضمان اهـ .
Then know that mutual oaths in sale are not restricted to disagreement over the price or the sold item; rather, they apply in every situation where each of them is a claimant or a denier, as mentioned in al-Kafi: He sold a female slave and they exchanged possession. Then the seller said, 'She belongs to Zaid; he ordered me to sell her.' And Zaid said, 'I sold her to you for one hundred dinars, and I took possession, and I sold your property.' So the item belongs to the buyer, and they take mutual oaths because the seller claims the authorization to sell, and the one to whom it is acknowledged (Zaid) denies; and the one to whom it is acknowledged claims the price from him, and he denies. If they swear, and it is unknown that she belongs to the one to whom it is acknowledged, and the buyer denies both of them, then the one who acknowledged (the seller) guarantees her value to the one to whom it is acknowledged. But if it is known that she belongs to the one to whom it is acknowledged, there is no guarantee.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage generalizes the application of mutual oaths (tahāluf) in sales. The author states that tahāluf is not limited to disputes over the price (thaman) or the subject matter (mabī'), but applies wherever both parties are simultaneously claimants and deniers. The example from al-Kafi illustrates this: A seller claims he sold on behalf of Zaid, while Zaid claims he sold directly. Here, the seller claims authorization (thus denying the buyer's property), Zaid claims the price (and denies the seller's authorization). Both have claims and denials, so they take mutual oaths. After oaths, if the true owner is unknown, the seller must pay the value to Zaid; if known, no guarantee. This shows that tahāluf applies in complex agency scenarios, not just simple price disputes.

Key Terms
مدعٍ ومنكر (muddaʿin wa munkir): A party who asserts a claim (muddaʿi) and one who denies (munkir); here both parties are muddaʿi and munkir simultaneously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, mutual oaths in a sale apply:
A) Only when the price is disputed.
B) Only when the subject matter is disputed.
C) In every situation where each party is both a claimant and a denier.
D) Only when the dispute is about the existence of the contract.

Key Takeaway
Mutual oaths (tahāluf) are not limited to price/commodity disputes; they apply whenever each party is simultaneously a claimant and a denier.

Order of Oaths in Mutual Disputes: Buyer or Seller First?
Determines which party's oath is taken first in mutual oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of oaths in mutual disputes over a sale, distinguish between disputes over the price and the subject matter, and explain the Hanafi proofs and the intra-madhhab refinement.

Core Idea
In mutual oaths, the buyer swears first when the dispute is over the price; the seller swears first when the dispute is over the subject matter.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā'iq clarifies the correct order of oaths in mutual disputes (tahāluf) between buyer and seller. It resolves an earlier difference among the Hanafi imams and provides the relied-upon position, which is refined by later scholars like al-Ramli. Understanding this ruling is crucial for judges and muftis handling sale disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the order of administering oaths when both parties differ over the sale. The ruling is that the buyer is sworn first in disputes over the price, and the seller first in disputes over the subject matter. This is a procedural wajib.
The ruling is not about who wins the case but the procedure to resolve the dispute.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary hadith cited is: 'When the two parties to a sale differ, the word is the seller's.' This hadith is used by Abu Yusuf initially to argue for the seller's oath first. Later, the stronger proof is the buyer's stronger denial and the benefit of nukūl (refusal to swear). Also, the Qasama hadith is used to limit the oath to negation only.
The hadith 'the word is the seller's' is interpreted as a general principle but is restricted in application.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The buyer is considered the stronger denier because he is first demanded to pay the price, so his denial is more critical. If he refuses to swear (nukūl), the seller's claim is established, which speeds up the process.
The focus is on the practical consequence of nukūl.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf initially held that the seller should swear first based on the hadith. Later, he retracted and agreed with Muhammad and the narration from Abu Hanifa that the buyer swears first when the dispute is over the price. This shows the process of tarjīḥ within the school.
The refinement by al-Ramli adds that if the dispute is over the subject matter, the seller swears first.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ and muftā bihī
The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is: buyer's oath first for price disputes, seller's oath first for subject-matter disputes. This is based on the later consensus of the Hanafi masters and the practical benefit of avoiding delay.
The judge is not free to choose arbitrarily; the specific order is prescribed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبدأ بيمين المشتري) وهذا قول محمد وأبي يوسف آخراً وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح، لأن المشتري أشدهما إنكاراً لأنه يطالب أولاً بالثمن أو لأنه يتعجل فائدة النكول وهو إلزام الثمن ولو بدأ بيمين البائع تتأخر المطالبة بتسليم المبيع إلى زمان استيفاء الثمن، وكان أبو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى يقول أولاً يبدأ بيمين البائع لقوله عليه السلام «إذا اختلف المتبايعان فالقول ما قاله البائع خصه بالذكر وأقل فائدته التقديم. أطلقه ببيع العين بالدين، أما في بيع العين بالعين أو الدين بالدين فالقاضي مخير للاستواء . وصفة اليمين أن يحلف البائع بالله تعالى ما باعه بألف ويحلف المشتري بالله ما اشتراه بألفين . وقال في الزيادات: يحلف بالله ما باعه بألف ولقد باعه بألفين ويحلف المشتري بالله ما اشتراه بألفين ولقد اشتراه بألف يضم الإثبات إلى النفي تأكيداً، والأصح الاقتصار على النفي لأن الأيمان على ذلك وضعت دل عليه حديث القسامة بالله ما قتلتم ولا علمتم له قاتلا». وفي شرح التلخيص من باب الاختلاف فيما يجب للبائع على المشتري وبالعكس مسألة الأصح فيها تقديم يمين البائع قوله : ( وفسخ القاضي بطلب أحدهما فلا ينفسخ البيع بحلفهما) لأنه لم قول المصنف : ( وبدأ بيمين المشتري قال الرملي : هذا إذا كان الاختلاف في الثمن، أما لو كان في المبيع يبدأ بيمين البائع كما يستفاد مما يأتي في الاختلاف في الإجارة تأمل اهـ . قلت : ووجهه أحدهما ومن نكل لزمه دعوى الآخر لأنه جعل باذلاً فلم يبق دعواه معارضاً لدعوى الآخر فلزم القول بثبوته، وهذا كله إذا كان الإختلاف في البدل مقصوداً، فإن كان في ضمن شيء كاختلافهما في الزق فلا تحالف والقول للمشتري في أنه الزق لأنه اختلاف في المقبوض والقول فيه قول القابض وتقدم بيانه في البيع الفاسد. ولم يذكر المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى حكم الاختلاف في الوصف وفيه تفصيل ؛ فإن كان في وصف الثمن تحالفا، وإن كان في وصف المبيع كما لو قال المشتري اشتريت هذا العبد على أنه كاتب أو خباز فقال البائع لم أشترط فالقول للبائع ولا تحالف؛ كذا في الظهيرية.
His saying: (And he begins with the oath of the buyer) — this is the saying of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf in his later view, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa, and it is correct, because the buyer is the more denying of the two, since he is first demanded for the price, or because he anticipates the benefit of nukul (refusal to swear) which is the imposition of the price. If one began with the seller's oath, the demand for delivery of the sold item would be delayed until the time of receiving the price. And Abu Yusuf used to say at first: begin with the seller's oath because of the Prophet's saying, 'When the two parties to a sale differ, the word is the seller's' — he specifically mentioned him, and the least benefit is priority. He left it unrestricted in a sale of a specific item for a debt. As for a sale of a specific item for a specific item, or debt for debt, the judge is free to choose due to equality. And the description of the oath: the seller swears 'by Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand,' and the buyer swears 'by Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand.' And it is said in al-Ziyadat: he swears 'by Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand, and indeed I sold it for two thousand,' and the buyer swears 'by Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand, and indeed I bought it for one thousand' — joining affirmation to negation for emphasis. The most correct is to restrict to negation because oaths are established that way, as indicated by the hadith of Qasama: 'by Allah, you did not kill nor did you know his killer.' And in Sharh al-Talkhis, from the chapter of disagreement over what is due to the seller from the buyer and vice versa, the matter: the most correct is to give priority to the seller's oath. His saying: (And the judge rescinds upon the request of one of them, so the sale is not rescinded by their taking oaths) because... The author's saying: (And he begins with the oath of the buyer). Al-Ramli said: This is if the disagreement is over the price; but if it is over the subject matter, he begins with the seller's oath, as derived from what will come in the disagreement in hire (ijara). Contemplate. End quote. I say: Its justification is one of them, and if one refuses, the claim of the other is binding because he is made a spender, so his claim no longer counters the other's claim, so the statement of its establishment is binding. And all this is if the disagreement is over the substitute (badal) as intended. But if it is within something, like their disagreement over the skin (ziqq), then there is no mutual oath, and the word is for the buyer that it is a skin because it is a disagreement over the received item (maqbud), and the word in that is for the receiver, as previously explained in the sale that is fasid. And the author did not mention the ruling on disagreement over description, and there is detail: if it is over the description of the price, they take mutual oaths; if over the description of the sold item, as if the buyer said, 'I bought this slave on condition that he is a scribe or baker,' and the seller said, 'I did not stipulate,' then the word is for the seller and there is no mutual oath; thus in al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling that the buyer's oath is taken first in mutual oaths (tahāluf). The author discusses the correct position: it is the view of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf in his later opinion, also narrated from Abu Hanifa, and is the correct one. The reason: the buyer is seen as the stronger denier (ashadduhumā inkāran) because he is first demanded to pay the price, or because he anticipates the benefit of the other's refusal (nukūl). If the seller swore first, the buyer's obligation to pay would be delayed. Abu Yusuf initially held that the seller should swear first, citing the hadith 'the word is the seller's' (al-qawlu mā qālahu al-bā'iʿ). The author refines this: the hadith applies generally in the sale of a specific item for a debt (ʿayn bi-dayn), but in other types (ʿayn bi-ʿayn or dayn bi-dayn) the judge may choose. The oaths' wording: negation only is sufficient, though some add affirmation for emphasis. The correct view is to limit to negation as per the Qasama hadith. Then the author quotes al-Ramli: buyer starts if the dispute is over price; if over the subject matter, seller starts. This is supported by examples from hire (ijāra). The commentary then explains what happens if one party refuses to swear (nukūl): the other's claim becomes binding. Finally, disputes over description: if it concerns the price description, mutual oaths; if the sold item's description (e.g., a skill attribute), the seller's word prevails without mutual oaths.

Key Terms
نكول (nukūl): Refusal of a party to take the oath; its consequence is that the opponent's claim is established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, whose oath is taken first when the dispute is over the price?
A) The seller's oath is taken first.
B) The buyer's oath is taken first.
C) The judge decides arbitrarily.
D) No oath is taken if the price is disputed.

Key Takeaway
In mutual oaths, the buyer swears first when the dispute is over the price; the seller swears first when the dispute is over the subject matter.
